HEADLINE: Officials Unraveling Woes of Texas Schools

BYLINE: By LAURA GRIFFIN

DATELINE: HUTCHINS, Tex., Aug. 26 

 BODY:
While other pupils around the state settled into the routine of classes, many Wilmer-Hutchins High School students spent their first week back at school sitting idly in the cafeteria, playing dominoes and taking naps.
 Their long-dilapidated school, unusable since it was flooded in summer storms, has become a symbol for what is being called the worst school district in the state. Five of the district's seven schools are in such bad shape that a state report recommends tearing them down.
 Now, Texas education officials are trying to determine how the district's financial situation deteriorated to the point that even with a $500,000 loan this summer, it could not afford to meet payroll for some of its teachers and staff members this week. 
 A grand jury looking into allegations of corruption in the district, which serves a mostly disadvantaged area five miles from the gleaming Dallas skyline, returned no decision this week but left the case open for further inquiry.
 Cedric Davis, the Wilmer-Hutchins school district police chief, took the accusations of wrongdoing and graft to the Dallas County district attorney's office this summer.
 ''They've mismanaged funds, and they're in the hole,'' Chief Davis said. ''Where'd that money go? It's not in the buildings or the students or the teachers.'' On Monday, the Texas Education Agency will begin an audit to determine what it will take to save the district. In connection with that audit, state health officials will inspect the schools for environmental and safety concerns, including air quality.
 ''They receive sufficient funds for operating a fairly well-achieving district,'' said Dr. Ed Flathouse, associate commissioner for finance and compliance at the education agency. ''But when you look at other districts, on the whole Wilmer-Hutchins comes up short every time.'' 
 Most agree something has to be done.
 No matter what happens, education officials say the 2,902 children in Wilmer-Hutchins schools are paying the price.
 ''Teachers and others may lose jobs, but a whole generation of youngsters in that district may be lost,'' Dr. Flathouse said. Superintendent Charles Matthews did not return repeated phone calls, but the Wilmer-Hutchins school board president, Luther Edwards, said the problems were caused more by decades of racial inequity and a lack of growth in the area than by the actions of the board or the administration. 
 Ninety-five percent of the students are black or Hispanic and 63 percent are economically disadvantaged.
 ''When they talk about mismanagement and poor leadership, why is it that it always happens in predominantly minority school districts?'' Mr. Edwards asked. ''It's a shame that in 2004 we're still grappling with the same thing -- racial tension -- but indirectly now.''
 But using race as an excuse offends some other African-Americans in the district. All along, they say, it has been a leadership issue. Chief Davis says that district money has paid salaries of people who did not work, contracts that should have been put out for bid but were not and for improper travel and phone expenses by trustees. 
 He also claims that the district illegally used federal money intended for instruction to pay bills, and he hopes to get the Justice Department involved.
 When Chief Davis took his accusations to district officials, he said, they retaliated by disbanding his department. He and officers have sued for wrongful termination, and a judge, granting a temporary injunction, reinstated the entire department.
 ''I'm angry that the kids have to go through this mess,'' said Chief Davis, who graduated from Wilmer-Hutchins in 1985 and whose daughter is a senior there. Mr. Edwards, the board president, also blamed a lack of financing from the state for the district's woes. But Wilmer-Hutchins gets more state and federal money than any other district in Dallas County. 
 Mr. Edwards also said he hoped voters would pass a $68 million bond package in September so work on the schools can begin.
 Turmoil in Wilmer-Hutchins goes back decades. In the 1990's, the district had four superintendents in less than five years and the state stepped in for two years in the latter part of the decade because of low student scores and money problems. 
 Johnny Brown, now DeKalb County superintendent in suburban Atlanta, ran the district during part of that time when test scores, school ratings and finances improved.
 ''All children can learn and excel, given the proper environment,'' he said. ''What's required of the adults is not rocket science -- it's to provide a quality education. I believe in local systems taking care of their own, but there are times, and this is one of them, that the state needs to step in.'' 
 It is not that easy, according to the state education agency. A school district must receive the lowest performance rating for two consecutive years before the agency can take it over. Wilmer-Hutchins is currently rated ''acceptable'' over all, despite low test scores among middle school and high school students.
 ''This district has a record of having the lowest to next-to-lowest rating,'' said Debbie Ratcliffe, a spokeswoman for the state agency. ''Right now, they're O.K. on that front, so that makes it difficult. But we can impose sanctions if there are financial problems.''
 Across the country, good school districts share a commonality -- their communities demand accountability and results.
 That has not happened in Wilmer-Hutchins. Historically, few people have participated in matters concerning the schools, local and state educators said.
 This time it is different, said Brenda Duff, P.T.A. president for the high school and Chief Davis's mother.
 ''They haven't demanded anything in the past, but I do see some changes coming,'' Ms. Duff said. ''The community's upset.''
 Many parents are trying to figure out ways to change districts, short of moving. And students say they are embarrassed.
 ''I'm afraid to claim this school, since it's the worst,'' said Reavis Skinner, 14, a freshman.
 A week before school was to start, the district announced that storm cleanup of the high school would delay classes for a week. 
 To Chief Davis, that storm was heaven sent -- without it, the problems may have gone unnoticed.
 ''The storms just enhanced a problem that has existed for a long time,'' he said. ''I think it was an act of God to bring this thing down.''

HEADLINE: Markets Extend Gains as Oil Prices Start to Cool

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

 BODY:
Stocks rose last week as the price of oil dropped almost 8 percent, to $43.18 a barrel, and a peace agreement was arranged in the Shiite holy city of Najaf in Iraq.
 The Dow Jones industrial average rose 84.87 points, or 0.8 percent, to 10,195.01, posting a gain for a third consecutive week. 
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index also had its third weekly gain in a row, climbing 9.42 points, or 0.9 percent, to 1,107.77.
 Boeing and Honeywell, both up 3 percent, led the Dow's climb, while Apple Computer, which rose 11.5 percent, and Hercules, a specialty chemical maker that gained 10.6 percent, led the S.&P. 500 higher. 
 The Nasdaq composite index rose for the second consecutive week, gaining 24.08 points, or 1.3 percent, to 1,862.09, led by Flextronics International, a provider of electronics manufacturing services. It rose 11.9 percent.
 Google fell in its first full week of trading. From a high on Monday of $109.40, Google dropped 3 percent by the end of the week, closing at $106.15 on Friday. The stock was still well above its debut price of $85. 
 In the bond market, the yield on the Treasury's 10-year note was at 4.23 percent, unchanged from the two previous Fridays, though not far from its lowest level since spring. JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

HEADLINE: The Street Of No Return

BYLINE: By STEVEN KURUTZ

 BODY:
Unified commercial strips are a hallmark of New York. There is the diamond district on West 47th Street and Restaurant Row on West 46th Street, and down on the Bowery, a series of storefronts offer all manner of lighting supplies. It stands to reason that it makes economic sense to dedicate a strip to that most final of purchases, the funeral. 
 In Chinatown, on Mulberry Street, between Bayard and Worth Streets, a kind of death row has sprung up; one narrow block is home to no less than three funeral homes, two flower shops, a monument maker, a coffin company and, most recently, a store that sells the incense and paper items burned in traditional Chinese funerals. 
 At the southern end of the street, like sturdy trunks from which the various businesses have sprouted, stand two of the funeral homes, Wah Wing Sang and Chinese Cheung Sang. Gordon L.W. Wong, the funeral director at Wah Wing Sang, said his business dated back 50 years and was the first on the block, followed by Cheung Sang and then NG Fook Funeral Service. As a mark of Wah Wing Sang's stature in the community, the so-called Godfather of Chinatown, Benny Ong, was laid out there in a widely attended ceremony in 1994. 
 Sensing a built-in clientele, the ancillary businesses (flower shops, gravestone makers) have followed the funeral homes' lead. Last fall, when the longstanding Italian restaurant Giambone closed, a coffin company moved in. The cottage industry came full circle in May with the addition of the Hing Tao Company. The tiny store sells incense and other ceremonial items like fake money and paper houses, which are burned at Chinese funerals so the deceased will have a place to live and money to spend in the afterlife. 
 For many years, the funeral homes were the main source for all the trappings of mourning, but Mr. Wong appeared unconcerned about the recent competition. ''We do a volume business,'' he said. 
 Which leads to odd juxtaposition for those who live and work on the block. Funerals take place all day long, and hearses and black Town Cars clog the street. The sidewalk is sprinkled with loose flowers. One resident described the unsettling feeling of eating lunch at Sam's Deli and seeing a new arrival being taken into a funeral home. 
 On one recent afternoon, a bell began to clang in front of Wah Wing Sang, and a coffin was loaded into a waiting hearse. Karl Gluck, walking to work nearby, stopped to watch. The funeral procession, he said, usually winds through the streets of Chinatown before driving on to the cemetery. 
 Had seeing so many funerals taught him anything about the ritual? ''The more important the person, the more black cars,'' he said. ''I don't know if that's true, but that's my observation anyway.''
 STEVEN KURUTZ

HEADLINE: Their Political Odyssey

BYLINE: By ALEX MINDLIN

 BODY:
LIKE many political candidates, Vinny Ignizio is immaculately dressed but poorly fed, accustomed to eating on the run. So it was not surprising one afternoon a few weeks ago to find the scrubbed, compact 30-year-old, who is running in a tough Republican primary on Staten Island for a state Assembly seat, sitting in his grandmother's curio-filled living room in Bensonhurst, Brooklyn, in a suit and tie, waiting for a pizza. 
 Nearby, his 55-year-old mother, Camille, discussed her own political loyalties. ''They're going to do what's right for the country,'' she said of the Republicans, her Brooklyn accent unmistakable despite 31 years of living on Staten Island. 
 Besides running in a Republican primary, Mr. Ignizio has served as the chief of staff for two Republican councilmen from Staten Island. For her part, Ms. Ignizio is a member of the Richmond County Republican Committee. The pair, who plan to vote for President Bush in November, are clearly among that minority of New Yorkers -- only 13 percent are registered Republicans -- who will feel right at home when the party's national convention starts tomorrow. 
 At the same time, these steadfast Republicans are heirs of a family with strong Democratic roots, a family populated by factory workers, ardent trade unionists and a Democratic state officeholder. In her youth, Ms. Ignizio marched in rallies for John F. Kennedy, hung out in the Democratic headquarters in Bensonhurst, and listened to union lore at her mother's knee.
 A series of complex forces propelled this New York family on their long political journey, forces that sound many of the major themes of New York and even national politics and have been traced in works like Samuel G. Freedman's 1996 book, ''The Inheritance: How Three Families and America Moved From Roosevelt to Reagan and Beyond.''
 For the Ignizios, as for so many families, one theme is that the power of Democratic ideals often diminishes as new generations succeed their immigrant forebears. Another is that Staten Island, where Camille and her husband moved in 1973, is the city's center of Republicanism. Yet another is that individual and sometimes random life experiences, from the woes of sweatshop life in the 30's to the burden of double-digit inflation in the 70's to the stench of a landfill in the 90's, are powerful shapers of personal politics.

Rooted in the Unions
 This family chronicle begins with Ms. Ignizio's mother, Mary Palmieri, long before Camille was born, at a time when Staten Island was mostly farmland. It was 1933 and Mary, a dark-eyed, rail-thin teenager, had moved with her family to Bensonhurst from a cold-water flat in Carroll Gardens. Now a chatty 83-year-old, she shed no tears over the move. ''That was a slum,'' Ms. Palmieri said of her former neighborhood. 
 During the war years, when factories were desperate for labor, Ms. Palmieri worked in the garment district. Her older sisters had told her horror stories about factory work. ''My oldest sister didn't have hours,'' Ms. Palmieri recalled. ''You just kept working until whatever hours they called for.'' There were no bathroom breaks, her sisters told her, and no lunch breaks or paid vacations.
 By the early 40's, unions had changed much of this. The International Ladies Garment Workers Union was at the height of its power, benefiting from tough new labor laws and close ties to the White House. ''If you had any problems,'' Ms. Palmieri said, ''you had someone in the place that took care of you.'' When a worker fought with her bosses, the union swooped in. No one knew what went on in the conferences that ensued, but the worker usually came out fine.
 In return, the union made its own demands. Ms. Palmieri anticipated some of them -- paying dues, participating in strikes -- but others caught her by surprise. In 1939, she and her coworkers were told to attend the World's Fair because someone important wanted it to succeed. ''You had to go,'' she said. ''Not,'' she added acidly, ''that they paid for it.''
 But she didn't resent the union urging its members to vote Democratic. ''To secure our jobs, we had to do it,'' Ms. Palmieri said. ''Otherwise the union would go down the drain.'' Individual candidates mattered little to her. ''We all said to each other, 'You better vote for Joe Schmo.'''
 In those years, when Bensonhurst was a Jewish and Italian neighborhood, Italian-Americans like Mary lived mostly in one- or two-family detached houses in the heart of the area. And like the Jews who lived in the apartment buildings along the elevated tracks, they had strong ties to the powerful Brooklyn Democratic machine, which delivered 65 percent of the borough vote for Franklin D. Roosevelt in 1940 and 1944. 
 In 1946, at a wedding, Ms. Palmieri met Danny Composto. Mr. Composto, a barber, was a Republican; he felt that Roosevelt had ''instigated'' the Japanese, and he nursed a grudge against the president for sending all his customers off to war. 
 Despite the couple's political differences, a courtship ensued. They were married in 1947, and in 1948 they had a daughter whom they named Camille and raised in Bensonhurst, in an apartment on quiet 79th Street, where they still live today.

Inflation Whips Camelot
 Camille was skinny like her mother, and a tomboy, fond of playing stickball with her younger brother. When she was 12, Kennedy was running for president, and Camille felt, as she put it, ''swept away.''
 ''Issues, who knew about issues?'' she said. ''Eisenhower was an old man to us. Everyone got into Kennedy and Camelot because they were young and vibrant.'' 
 Mr. Composto and his daughter, decked out in her political buttons, used to clash over politics at the dinner table. To his chagrin, Bensonhurst in 1960 was even more Democratic than it had been two decades earlier, so much so that the Democratic club was a hangout for neighborhood children. 
 Family ties as much as neighborhood ethos drew Camille to the Democrats. Her grandfather's cousin, Frank Composto, had been a state assemblyman, a state senator and a State Supreme Court judge, all on the Democratic line. ''Here we had a relative who was a politician,'' Camille said. ''Seeing his posters around, I think that's what made me register as a Democrat. My mother used to say the Democratic Party was for the working people, so that made me proud.''
 Though her father wanted her to go to college, Ms. Ignizio went to work as a typist and switchboard operator at Manufacturers Hanover Bank, on 49th Street in Manhattan. She loved it. ''Most of us were 19, 20 years old, and there were some recently married people. Everybody would kid around with each other.'' 
 At a cousin's college graduation party, she met Vince Ignizio, and they saw each other often during the long subsequent summer at Manhattan Beach, when all of Bensonhurst seemed to be stretched out on beach blankets. One day Camille's cousin called, saying, ''One of my friends wants to ask you out on a date.'' Camille was cautious. ''Which one?'' she demanded. 
 Two years later, in 1970, Vince and Camille were married in St. Bernadette's Roman Catholic Church, on 83rd Street in Bensonhurst, where Camille had been baptized and confirmed. Camille was 22, Richard Nixon was president, and the nation was entering a recession in which 600,000 people in New York State alone would lose their jobs by year's end.
 When Vince met Camille, he was working on the paving gang at Kennedy Airport. By the time they married, he had been promoted to operations agent at the airport, helping to oversee everything land-bound, from luggage trucks to runway repair crews. If a plane crashed during takeoff or landing, Vince was often the first on the scene, sometimes dodging pieces of flying metal. ''I saw a lot of dead bodies,'' he said. 
 But despite Vince's promotions, money was tight, for these were the years of double-digit inflation. ''We couldn't afford a house in Brooklyn,'' Camille remembered, ''especially in Bensonhurst. It was too high out here for us. We tried Long Island, and the taxes were too high out there.'' 
 Finally, the couple settled for a semi-attached three-bedroom in Eltingville, on the South Shore of Staten Island. It was cheap and, importantly for Vince, close to all three major airports. Staten Island at the time was almost rural, only opened to mass colonization a decade before via the Verrazano-Narrows Bridge. The island had so few stores, Camille went to Bensonhurst every week to buy groceries. 
 With her husband working nights and studying for a bachelor's degree during the day, Camille was lonely. She was left at home with her daughter, Theresa, born in 1971, and her son Vinny, born three years later. ''Those were hard years,'' she said. ''I'm not going to lie.'' She remembers the Christmas morning Vince got held up at the airport, where he had worked the Christmas Eve midnight shift. Unwilling to start the holiday without him, or even to let the children glimpse their presents, Camille kept them in bed until 11 a.m. ''I said, 'Let me go down and see if Santa Claus is here.' Then I said, 'Don't you want some oatmeal?'''
 As a young mother at home, Camille had time to watch the news, a habit she had picked up from Vince, who liked to shout at the television set during the evening broadcast. In 1979, with the annual inflation rate at 11 percent -- double what it was on her wedding day -- Camille began to develop a distaste for President Jimmy Carter. ''You start becoming conscious of things when you're raising a young family,'' she explained. ''Money gets tight, and you start putting your situation together with what's going on in the world.'' She was depending heavily on her credit cards to buy clothes for the children, and there was never enough money for family outings. 
 Camille was also disgusted with her country's weakness abroad, such as the botched rescue of American hostages in Iran in 1980. ''When the idiots went in there and tried to get them out with those stupid helicopters,'' she said, ''they all crashed into one another and they ended up killing more soldiers.'' She started to ask herself: ''Why is the country in the mess it's in?'' 
 The answer, in her eyes, was Mr. Carter. Outside, watching her children play on the lawn, Camille used to argue about politics with her neighbors, some of them unionized city workers and staunch Democrats. ''They thought Jimmy Carter was for the people,'' Camille said. ''Rosalynn Carter runs around the White House in curlers, and all that.'' Camille, by contrast, considered the president wishy-washy.
 In 1980, she voted for Ronald Reagan, the first Republican presidential candidate she had ever supported. Her husband went through a similar change of political heart, and so even did her mother, the onetime champion of unions. ''When Reagan came along,'' Camille said, ''you knew he wasn't going to take any nonsense from anybody.'' 
 Camille has voted Republican in every presidential election since 1980. Once, she believed that the Republicans were the party of the rich; after Reagan's election, she felt ''the Republicans were helping the people'' by cutting inflation. ''That helped us a lot,'' she said, ''because in those days it felt like we were always behind the eight ball.''
 In fact, like many Americans, she grew to adore Reagan. When the hostages in Iran were released, she stood in her living room and screamed with joy. Vinny, a boy of 6 when Reagan was elected, remembers being allowed to stay up late for his speeches. ''The whole house stopped,'' he recalled. 

Life in 'The Forgotten Borough'
 Vinny was a wild child; ''If you'd been my oldest child,'' his mother used to tell him, ''you'd be my only child.'' Growing up in the 70's and 80's, he had a country kid's childhood, playing with friends in the woods and riding bikes along makeshift trails. They gave little thought to the local political issues that consumed their parents, like the Staten Island secessionist movement and the Fresh Kills landfill, which aroused considerable fury. (Vinny did know about the existence of the landfill, which was two miles from his home. On summer nights, when the wind blew the wrong way, he would ask his mother to turn on the air-conditioner because his room stank.) 
 By 1981, when Vinny was 7, his father had become a supervisor at the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey's downtown heliport. Vinny got to hang around helicopters and the celebrities they transported, like former Mayor Edward I. Koch and former Gov. Thomas H. Kean of New Jersey, currently the chairman of the Sept. 11 commission. When these grandees emerged from beneath the rotors, they'd ruffle Vinny's hair before settling into their limousines. Former Gov. Mario Cuomo liked to heft Vinny onto his shoulders. ''Subconsciously,'' Vinny said, ''I thought: 'How wonderful it would be to be those people. How proud my mother and father would be.'''
 Politics ranked low among Vinny's interests in high school, but he was absorbing Staten Islanders' conservatism and their antipathy toward the Democrats running the city, who they felt had slighted the borough. When he was a student at Rider University in Lawrenceville, N.J., he found himself arguing politics with a fiercely liberal professor. ''The professor would say, 'The poor people of the world are held down by the rich people,''' Vinny recalled. He would reply: ''I don't believe that. My dad started out literally sweeping airports.'' The professor responded: ''When you have your own class, you can hand out whatever you want to. Or on your own, you can be an activist.'' 
 Vinny took the suggestion. In his junior year, he joined the Richmond County Republican Committee, and began working on campaigns on Staten Island. He was particularly taken with Vito J. Fossella, now a congressman. ''Vito had just won the City Council, and Vito was young,'' Vinny recalled. ''I thought, 'Hey, young guys are getting involved.''' Soon after graduating from college, he became chief of staff for Stephen J. Fiala, a Republican councilman from his own South Shore neighborhood, whose campaign he had managed. Today, he holds the same position in the office of Mr. Fiala's successor, Andrew J. Lanza.
 Four years after Vinny went to work for Mr. Fiala, the Fresh Kills landfill officially closed. Vinny's eyes widen at the memory. ''I honestly thought it would never close,'' he said. ''When I went to college, people would say: 'Where are you from? Oh, Staten Island. That's where the garbage is.'''

Still Afloat, Still Republican
 Sitting in a pizzeria near City Hall in late July, Vinny ticked off the indignities visited upon Staten Island by Democrats. First, the dump. ''It just kept expanding and expanding under Democratic regimes,'' he said, until it was closed by Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani and Gov. George E. Pataki. Then the infrequent visits of Mayor David N. Dinkins. ''He never hid his disdain for our borough.'' Then the scarcity of services like express buses and sewers. ''To some people, that's foreign,'' Vinny said. ''You know, 'What do you mean you don't have sewers?' Well, when you're the forgotten borough, you're last on the list.''
 It is voters who share these feelings who have made Staten Island New York's most Republican borough. Staten Island is 37.8 percent Republican; the next most Republican borough, Queens, is only 18.1 percent Republican. 
 As Vinny talked, the fellow sliding a pizza into the oven recognized him. ''How's Staten Island?'' he asked. ''Doing good,'' Vinny said with a practiced air. ''Still afloat.'' 
 Vinny has had plenty of chances to work on his banter recently. He is in a three-way race for the Republican nomination in the South Shore's 62nd Assembly District, extraordinary for a party that seldom needs to hold primaries in this largely Democratic city. Every night, Vinny and his wife of eight months, Letizia, who is also a Republican, go from house to house, introducing themselves to the neighbors. ''We'll do anything to get him elected,'' said Letizia, an aide in the county clerk's office.
 If they succeed, Camille's family will have a striking way to measure its political journey. Vinny will be sitting in the same Assembly chamber where his great-grandfather's cousin once sat. But he will be across the aisle, on the Republican side.

HEADLINE: When Renovation Is Spelled R-e-s-p-e-c-t

BYLINE: By PENELOPE GREEN

 BODY:
MAX FRIEDMAN and Tom Romich are revolutionaries when it comes to New York City real estate. Describing the work they've done on their 1834 Greenwich Village town house, they use words like ''patience'' and ''respect'' -- shocking notions in an age of frenzied, instant and total renovation. 
 ''You can't barrel in and impose yourself on a house,'' said Mr. Friedman, who is 51 and a partner in the real estate division of the Pillsbury Winthrop law firm. ''You have to live in it a while, and be patient.'' 
 Patience he knows: as counsel to the New York City Economic Development Corporation, Mr. Friedman has been involved in the two decades -- that's four mayoral administrations -- of Times Square's redevelopment. 
 Mr. Romich, 56, director of institutional advancement at the New York School of Interior Design, said: ''You have to let the house speak to you. It's only by living in a house that you understand where the light is, for example, and how it changes as the seasons change.'' 
 This is why the renovation of their Federal-style row house, which they bought in 1993 for just over $1 million, is only now complete, the result of no hodgepodge of improvements nor agonizing dismantling but a mindful grand design, created by the architect Andrew Freireich and his associate, Adam Platt, about five years ago and acted upon two years ago (the contracting firm was Broad Channel Builders). 
 Counting Mr. Friedman and Mr. Romich, there have been just four owners in the last century, and each tenancy resulted in various embellishments -- zigs or zags in the house's stylistic history -- all of which the two wanted to acknowledge and archive in their renovation. 
 In the 20's and 30's, a lawyer and his wife, a sculptor, were in residence. A glorious, ur-Greenwich Village rooftop studio, with a massive, peaked skylight-cum-roof, was their grandest contribution. (At the Buildings Department, Mr. Friedman found a permit request from this period, for a hoist to cantilever out over the street from the studio, the better to get art in and out. It was denied, he said.) They also threw in a ''modern'' master bathroom, with that era's totemic salmon American Standard fixtures. 
 To Mr. Freireich's amusement, this was saved and relocated to the third floor, and tiled in an abstracted form of the traditional basket-weave pattern. ''It was important enough to someone in the 1930's, so . . . '' Mr. Friedman said, his voice trailing off. 
 He'll show you the little late 19th-century marble sink in its cupboard in his third-floor office, and say in the same apologetic tone, ''It's kind of idiotic, but somebody went to the trouble of putting it in, so we restored it.'' 
 In the 1950's and 60's, the house was owned by a glassmaker-turned-prominent-art-collector and his wife, who was the sister of a prominent sculptor. 
 ''I read somewhere that the house was 'the epicenter of the Greenwich Village art world,' '' said Mr. Friedman, who described the puzzling furrows and channels they found in the house's wall-to-wall carpeting (now long gone). They realized quickly these were the marks from that sculptor's installations. 
 A midcentury-modern chandelier of colored glass cylinders was mostly smashed save for two cylinders. They've refashioned one, in bright turquoise blue, into a single hanging light in the center of the parlor floor. Both eyed it quizzically the other day, pondering whether the white one, now lying fallow on the marble mantel -- an 1860's addition -- might look more appropriate. 
 Do not be fooled into thinking this archival approach has resulted in a four-story version of Miss Haversham's attic. ''What we did was restore where possible,'' Mr. Freireich said, ''recreate where appropriate and reinterpret when necessary.'' 
 Mr. Romich said, ''What we did was let the light back in.'' 
 Like most town houses in New York, this one was a collection of little rooms on many floors. On the ground and parlor floors, the various owners had pushed out a wall here, or covered up a window there, so the jumble of small spaces was even more pronounced. 
 Mr. Freireich's design became a kind of excavation. He opened up the space on both floors and connected the floors at the back with a broad interior staircase. He used detailing and color to extend the feeling of space and marry the different levels.
 The ground floor is laid with Portuguese limestone, which Mr. Platt reconfigured in umpteen ways to make the pattern break just right around the landings, the stairs and the hearths, and also to echo the shape of the bluestone they'd harvested from the garden and repaved half the yard with, and the slate slab sidewalk on the street side. ''It was a scale thing,'' Mr. Freireich said. ''There were all these elements that had to work together and that took a fair amount of doing.'' 
 Though the house had had its Beaux-Arts period and its Deco stage, it was its Federal beginnings that Mr. Freireich reached back for: a light bright aesthetic that nods to the neo-Classical. Some details were historically accurate, like the new wide white pine planks on the parlor floor. 
 ''We found plywood under the wall-to-wall carpet, and underneath that were the remnants of the parquet floor from the 30's, and underneath that were the original pine planks,'' Mr. Friedman said, explaining that the pine was scarred with nail holes from the parquet. These were planed off and recycled onto the third- and fourth-floor hallways. Other details, like the simple cornices over the bank of French doors at the back -- arrayed to match the six-over-six mullioned windows they've added, a landmark detail in modern, double-glazed form -- were more conceptual than historical. 
 ''Those are no style,'' Mr. Freireich said. ''Those are my 'appropriate' style.'' 
 There are relics of the house's earlier lives -- ''the footsteps on the stairs,'' as Mr. Friedman said -- that have not found their way into the public spaces. One snowy day seven years ago, they were cleaning out a cupboard upstairs when they came upon a large metal object that looked remarkably like . . . a grenade. They debated a bit, and finally called in the bomb squad, which confirmed that this particular souvenir was, in fact, authentic. 
 ''Our block was in lockdown for four hours on the coldest day of the year,'' Mr. Romich said, ''which was when we learned what great neighbors we had. They said, 'Come inside and have some coffee while they take care of your bomb!' '' 
 Samantha, their adopted standard poodle, arrived two years ago. A suburban dog, she was slow to master the stairs, which she now takes three risers at a time. 
 Mr. Romich comes from five generations of Ohio farmers and grew up in the house that's been in his family since 1872. Mr. Friedman, whose parents met in Siberia, was born in Paris and raised in Brooklyn. They have been together for 24 years; this is the second house they've restored. Their backgrounds couldn't be more different, Mr. Friedman said, or more similar. ''I think we each have a sense of the value of simplicity,'' he said. ''And a respect for what has preceded us, and brought us to this place.''

HEADLINE: The Rising Appeal (and Falling Cost) of Term Insurance

BYLINE: By CARLA FRIED

 BODY:
WHEN it comes to life's priorities, protecting one's family falls in the priceless category. And because of increased competition, the actual price for term life insurance has dropped by half in a decade. For $40 or so a month -- about the cost of a cup of coffee a day -- a healthy 40-year-old male can buy a policy that will pay $500,000 if he dies within 20 years.
 And cheaper means more popular. Term policies accounted for 22 percent of the $11.8 billion in total premiums paid by individuals in 2003, up from 13 percent in 1993, according to the Life Insurance Marketing and Research Association. But those figures do not capture the new dominance of term insurance because term premiums are much lower than those for other types of life insurance. 
 The drop in term premiums, fueled in part by consumers' ability to comparison shop online, is spurring increased demand among new customers as well as policy holders jumping at the chance to switch to less expensive policies. Five years ago, Joseph Frawley Jr., a retired police sergeant in Cambridge, Mass., bought a 20-year policy with a $1 million death benefit that cost $550 a month. That high premium reflected a bout with melanoma and a history of cancer in his family. 
 ''It was an onerous cost,'' said Mr. Frawley, who now operates his own corporate security firm, Eastern Security Inc. 
 Working with AccuQuote, an online service that compares insurance policies, Mr. Frawley, 52, recently got a new 20-year, $1 million policy for $330 a month. 
 Term insurance is the most basic form of life insurance. It provides coverage for a fixed period; if the policyholder dies in that time, the beneficiary collects the benefit. At term's end, the policy has no value. By contrast, with far more costly universal life and whole life policies, the beneficiary receives the payout whenever the policyholder dies. Such policies include an investing component, known as the cash value. 
 J.Robert Hunter, director of insurance at the Consumer Federation of America, said term was the logical choice for most people, once they understand its sole purpose. 
 ''Life insurance is a bad name,'' he said. ''It should be called insurance to protect against the economic consequences of premature death. If that goal is made clear, then all you need is a sufficient amount of coverage for a specific period of time.''
 Mr. Hunter had term policies with a combined death benefit of $2 million while he was raising his six children. Now that they are grown, Mr. Hunter, 68, no longer has life insurance.
 Unlike universal or whole life policies, which remain difficult to decipher, it is easy to compare prices when shopping for term insurance. Web sites like Insurance.com,Insure.com, AccuQuote.com and Term4Sale.com provide price quotations from a basket of insurers. Or, an independent agent or fee-only insurance adviser can do the shopping for you.
 An insurer will use your personal data to determine your risk profile, much the way a mortgage lender uses your credit rating. Though each insurer has its own system, there are generally three broad categories: standard, preferred and preferred plus (for the lowest-risk, healthiest policyholders). 
 As insurers look for ways to bolster the bottom line, some are tightening qualifications for the least-expensive categories.
 ''The price you are first quoted is not the same as the price you may end up qualifying for,'' said David Mendels, who runs Creative Financial Concepts, a financial planning firm in New York. Between that initial quotation and a final offer, your medical records will be scoured. If you want a death benefit of $500,000 or more, the insurer is likely to require a blood sample. The higher the benefit -- and your age -- the likelier the insurer will demand a full medical exam.
 ''Even superhero marathon runners may have a hard time qualifying for the best rates,'' said James Van Elsen, a consulting actuary in Pella, Iowa, who advises insurers. He said that while 30 percent of an insurer's pool might have previously qualified for the best rates, he believed that as few as about 10 percent might make the cut today. 
 Byron J. Udell, chief executive of AccuQuote, said that anyone who was bumped down from preferred plus to preferred could face a 25 percent premium increase. A standard policy can be at least double the rate of preferred plus. 
 Ten years ago, for example, a 40-year-old man, 6 feet tall and weighing 230 pounds, might have qualified for the best rate. Today, however, a 40-year-old man would need to weigh no more than 200 pounds or so to get the lowest premium. Insurers are also looking more closely at cholesterol readings. A decade ago, a count of no more than 240 with a cholesterol-to-L.D.L. ratio of 6.5 or lower could snag the best rate. Now you often need a count of no more than 205 and a ratio of 4.5 or less. L.D.L., or low-density lipoprotein, is the kind of cholesterol associated with heart disease.
 Genetics can be important, too. The health histories of your siblings, as well as those of your parents, are now part of the analysis. The onset of a serious disease in a family member is enough to boot you out of contention for the best rate.
 Your performance behind the wheel is another factor. Some insurers now charge a higher premium if you have more than one moving violation. 
 ''Knowing which companies are tighter or more lenient on certain issues is the key to ultimately getting the best policy,'' Mr. Udell said. ''Not all underwriters have the same rules, and it's impossible for a consumer to know what those guidelines are.'' 
 Buying the right amount of insurance, of course, is also important. David Barkhausen, a fee-only life insurance adviser in Lake Bluff, Ill., says people tend to underestimate their family's financial needs and buy too little insurance. A conservative approach, he said, is to buy enough insurance to allow the beneficiary to live off the interest income from the death benefit, and not have to touch the principal. For example, if you assume an annual return of 5 percent, you should buy insurance equal to 20 times the annual income you want to replace. 
 ''But if you have built up other assets, you can use a smaller insurance amount,'' Mr. Barkhausen said. 
 Another factor is the length of time your beneficiaries will need that income. Do you have toddlers and want to provide for their education costs through graduate school? Or are your children finished with school? 
 Glenn Daily, a fee-only insurance consultant in New York, recommends that his clients spend $149 for the ESPlanner software (esplanner.com), which produces a personal financial analysis.
 The goal, experts say, should be to have the insurance in place until you have other assets -- pensions, Social Security, retirement funds -- that can cover your expenses. 
 A NEW strain of term insurance, called return of premium, is getting mixed reviews. The pitch is that if you are still alive at the end of the term you will be repaid all your premiums. But those premiums can be more than 30 percent costlier than a standard policy, and you don't get the money back until the end of the policy -- and you get nothing if you let the policy lapse. 
 Mr. Udell at AccuQuote.com says such policies can make sense for someone who doesn't like the idea of paying for insurance and getting nothing in return. ''With basic term insurance you only get paid if you die; this allows you to get something back if you in fact survive the policy.'' But, he added, you must consider carefully whether you could do better investing the difference in premiums on your own, and whether you have the resolve to keep paying the premium for the entire term.
 Some critics dismiss these policies as marketing ploys. ''I'm just skeptical of anything where I have to tie up my money for that long a period,'' Mr. Daily said. 
 Joseph M. Belth, professor emeritus of insurance at Indiana University and editor of The Insurance Forum, a newsletter, said: ''It's a gimmick. You don't buy insurance to get money back. You buy insurance to protect your family.''

QUICK READ
For peace of mind when buying term insurance, know the terminology.

GUARANTEED LEVEL TERM
Refers to a constant level of premiums over time. But make sure that the guarantee lasts the entire term of the insurance; a 10-year guarantee on a 20-year policy exposes you to premium shocks halfway into the term.

CONVERTIBILITY
Allows you to convert a term policy to a permanent one. But keep track of when the provision expires; don't expect the insurer to follow up. Ask what permanent policies will be available.

RIGHT OF RE-ENTRY
This clause in some policies refers to your right to get more insurance once the original term expires. Find out the terms of re-entry. You don't want to have to qualify again.

INSURER FINANCIAL STRENGTH RATING
Your policy is basically an investment in the future of the insurance company - specifically, its ability to pay your beneficiaries. One expert recommends that you choose an insurer with high ratings from three of the five rating firms: A. M. Best, Fitch, Moody's, Standard & Poor's and Weiss. Most insurance agents and Web sites have this information.

HEADLINE: If Minimum Wage Rises, Will Anyone Notice?

BYLINE: By WARREN STRUGATCH

 BODY:
EXACTLY how much do Long Island's lowest-paid workers earn? Or, to put it more bluntly, how little?
 The federal government says the answer either way is $5.15 an hour. That's the minimum wage, and it tallies up to $206 a week, full time. Can anyone survive on that in Nassau or Suffolk? 
 Anecdotal evidence, including comments made by employers, business groups and entry-level workers, suggests hardly anyone does or needs to. The de facto Long Island minimum hourly rate, it seems, wears the face of Alexander Hamilton. 
 Five dollars 15 cents an hour, or $10? The difference could be significant as the campaign to raise the minimum wage heats up. Across the country, advocates for the working poor and various pro-labor groups promote raising the minimum wage in order to lift millions out of dire poverty. Their efforts have met fierce resistance from employer groups, which argue that paying untrained workers more will generate demand for higher pay up the ladder, too, causing inflation.
 The campaign is strong in New York, and earlier this year succeeded in getting legislation passed in both houses that would have raised the minimum wage incrementally to $7.15 by 2007. The measure was vetoed by Governor Pataki earlier this month.
 Despite the recent setback, advocates vowed to continue their efforts. Right after the Pataki veto, the Working Families Party, a labor-supported advocacy group based in Brooklyn, organized a rally in Mineola and four other locations statewide. 
 The Long Island Association says that an increase in the minimum wage isn't needed. The poorest workers already earn more than the government minimum, thanks to the law of supply and demand, the group says.
 Pearl Kamer, the organization's chief economist, dismissed the government minimum as implausible. Why? Anyone earning minimum wage, she said, could not afford food, clothing or housing in this region.
 Dr. Kamer said that even Long Island's poorest workers can, and do, hold out for more. ''On Long Island no one is willing to work for minimum wage, given the labor shortage we have on both the top end and the low end of the market,'' she said. As a result, the de facto minimum wage on Long Island is ''between $8 and $10,'' she said.
 Others organizations studying the issue have arrived at contrary conclusions. The Working Families Party analyzed Bureau of Labor Statistics reports and contends that about 97,000 people in Nassau and Suffolk would benefit from a $7.15 minimum. The Fiscal Policy Institute, a nonpartisan research organization in Latham, N.Y., analyzed data from the Census Bureau's Current Population Survey, released in March, and found that 8 percent of Nassau's work force and 7.3 percent of Suffolk's live on less than $7.15 an hour. 
 While the Fiscal Policy Institute's position contradicts Dr. Kamer's, the organization echoed her reasoning. ''The prevailing minimum wage is not high enough to afford a two-bedroom home at fair-market rent anywhere in America,'' the institute said in a statement. On Long Island, it isn't enough for a rented room with a hotplate.
 Hard data are elusive. Neither the federal nor state labor departments track hourly wage levels. Nor do the Long Island Association or other regional business groups. Consequently, evidence cited by both sides is anecdotal and speculative.
 Gary Huth, an economist with the State Labor Department in Hicksville, staked out a position midway between the nobody-versus-multitudes polarities. While some Long Islanders do work for minimum wage, he said, it is probably not a large number of people, and employers pay above the minimum wage to recruit workers. 
 People on both sides of the argument agree on at least one thing: unskilled workers on Long Island today are increasingly immigrants.
 Irv Miljoner, the Long Island district director for the federal Labor Department's wage and hourly work division, said minimum-wage work was increasingly the province of those newly arrived in this country, who presumably have fewer options than other workers. 
 ''Overwhelmingly, those jobs today involve immigrants,'' Mr. Miljoner said, describing unskilled labor. Newcomers, he said, are disproportionately vulnerable to compensation abuses. 
 Union organizers have focused recruitment efforts increasingly on newcomers in recent years. Jose Alvarez, the acting president of the Long Island branch of the A.F.L.-C.I.O, in Farmingdale, said the union's organizing efforts increasingly focus on immigrants in such industries as retail, tourism and agriculture. Minimum-wage workers survive, he said, by rooming in motels, several families squeezing into rooms intended for one or two people. Often these workers are paid, illegally, less than the minimum wage, he said.
 ''This is widespread across Long Island among people working for the minimum wage,'' Mr. Alvarez said. ''This is the hidden America.''
 While immigrants moving into minimum-wage jobs is a familiar urban phenomenon, historically that has not been so on Long Island. In Nassau and Suffolk, students worked not so much from necessity as from the desire to afford trendy clothes, cash to go out on dates, cars and other luxuries. They endured the summer elbow grease and after-school drudgery knowing their professional career paths would soon afford them much better.
 That Archie-and-Veronica world has disappeared, gone the way of rabbit ears and pocket calculators.
 Today, a combination of parental affluence and the students' own precocious careerism effectively rule out low-paying part-time jobs, Dr. Kamer said.
 ''Students would rather work unsalaried in a position in their future field, as interns, rather than make minimum wage cooking hamburgers,'' she said. In most cases, ''their parents agree with them,'' she said.
 She acknowledged that the trend has been cited, less benignly, by those asserting that America's youth has grown soft. ''They aren't lazy at all,'' Dr. Kamer said. ''They feel it isn't necessary to work these jobs.''
 The immigrants who have succeeded them earn more in a day on Long Island, mopping floors, irrigating lawns and cleaning swimming pools than they might have in a month in such homelands as Guatemala and Ecuador.
 Last year, word of Long Island largess reached Eric Rodriguez, a 37-year-old Honduran living in Texas.
 Mr. Rodriguez said he was living in Houston with green card status, earning $7 an hour. A Honduran friend he knew from Texas phoned from Glen Cove, where he had moved. The friend bubbled with stories about Long Island prosperity, and the general availability of $10-an-hour jobs.
 Mr. Rodriguez, a mechanic by training, hurriedly made plans and headed for Glen Cove. He too now earns $10 an hour repairing auto and boat engines, meeting potential employers each morning at the city-run shape-up site on Sea Cliff Avenue. Some days, with overtime, he brings home $100, or more. The steady employment allows Mr. Rodriguez to send $200 back home every month to support his two small sons.
 ''There is a lot of money on Long Island,'' Mr. Rodriguez said, speaking in Spanish. Switching to English, he added: ''New York is a good life.''

HEADLINE: Make Way for The Big Duck

BYLINE: By WILLIAM McSHANE. 
William McShane is president of the Long Island Business Aviation Association.

DATELINE: FARMINGDALE 

 BODY:
IT'S time to move Long Island's classic roadside icon, the Big Duck, to this region's newest entryway for hundreds of thousands of visitors -- MacArthur Airport in Bohemia.
 Once considered a traveler's curiosity, the wood and concrete duck was originally intended to greet the motorists traveling to Long Island in the 1930's, when Sunrise Highway was the principal artery and agriculture (like duck farms) was the predominant industry. Roadside stands would try almost anything to attract the attention of New York visitors out for a ride in the countryside, and competition was fierce. An example of popular marketing at the time and kitsch culture, the Big Duck was a contemporary of roadside architecture like huge apples, Freudian frankfurters and Paul Bunyons that towered above the Studebakers and Ford flivers. The Big Duck was the 10-ton front office where Martin Maurer sold ducks and eggs fresh from his farm in Riverhead. 
 Today these cultural artifacts of a bygone era are as rare as, well, duck teeth. Most disappeared in the 1950's, when new interstate highways made the idea of a casual drive along rural roads to get from here to there a curious oddity. As the tempo of American life revved into high gear, no one had the time or patience to pull over for a two-story Native American, a concrete ice cream cone or a similar attraction. Yet Long Island's Big Duck survived the wrecker's ball, decades of indifference and its current move four miles down the road in Flanders. Today it is a registered national landmark, efficiently managed by Friends for Long Island's Heritage, and protected from all those who would seek its rendering with a bulldozer.
 What it is not protected from is indifference. Currently tucked away in Suffolk County's Sears Bellows County Park, the Big Duck is better known by Long Island trivia aficionados than by the general public it was originally designed to entice. There are preliminary discussions supposedly with Southampton officials to return the Big Duck to its previous site, but that won't alter its modest visitation numbers.
 Suffolk County's town and county leadership should be pondering how they can best celebrate the history of the Big Duck. If they want to ensure that as many visitors as possible actually see it, then they should move the structure to the one place where it is guaranteed an audience of hundreds of thousands of travelers to and from Long Island: MacArthur Airport, where Southwest Airlines and the Town of Islip opened the new $55 million Peter J. McGowan Concourse earlier this month. 
 By transporting the Big Duck to the airport, we would acknowledge that this concrete fowl isn't some dusty curio from another era but a dynamic and relevant participant in a multibillion-dollar tourism industry now crucial to the economic well being of Long Island. And by taking its place at MacArthur, the Big Duck would reclaim its status as a highly visible landmark for travelers and reinforce its role as a fun icon for visitors. The Big Duck even has the potential to become a tourist attraction in its own right, and its new setting at MacArthur Airport would help spread the news about the airport's new terminal.
 The move will cost at least $60,000, but taxpayers need not pay for it. If the airport is as enthusiastic about having the Big Duck as a gateway as it is about its expanded flight schedule, it might consider using a portion of increased passenger revenue to pay for the move. 
 The biggest obstacles to relocating the Big Duck to such a visible location are inertia, confusion over jurisdiction and the fondness that some on the East End may have for the giant bird. In the end those who control the duck's fate must realize that effective protection of our heritage requires that our icons and artifacts be seen and appreciated by as many people as possible. 
 The Big Duck has always been meant to amuse, attract and entertain. It can't do that job sitting on a quiet backwater road. Let's give the Big Duck a boarding pass to MacArthur Airport.


GRAPHIC: Photo: Martin Maurer's famous duck at its current location. (Photo by Ed Betz/The New York Times) 

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: In Cleveland's Suburbs, New Luxury vs. Old

BYLINE: By LISA CHAMBERLAIN

DATELINE: SHAKER HEIGHTS, Ohio 

 BODY:
BUILT nearly 100 years ago as one of the country's first planned communities, this is still in many ways an ideal community. Old-growth trees shade stately English Tudor, Country French and Georgian homes. Lush green lawns gently slope toward winding residential streets. Well-maintained rapid transit lines whisk people to downtown Cleveland in less than 20 minutes. The public school system serves an economically and racially diverse student body while remaining one of the best in Ohio. Exclusive private schools, lured by the area's founders with free land, are still in operation today.
 But these houses are not as popular as they once were. While moderately priced homes in Shaker Heights still sell at a rapid clip, more expensive homes are moving slowly, and for lower prices than owners imagined they might get. ''I wouldn't say the values have gone down, but they've stagnated,'' said Daniel P. Forrester, a vice president at Michael S. Folkman & Associates, who estimates that he conducts about 40 percent of all home appraisals over $1 million in the Greater Cleveland market. ''There have not been a lot of sales in the million or more range lately.'' 
 Shaker Heights has long been one of the most desirable and well-known suburbs in the Midwest. But these old houses don't have the vast kitchens and great rooms that today's buyers want -- and having the highest property tax rate in the state doesn't help either. Meanwhile, custom-built houses of equal square footage with high-tech amenities near downtown Cleveland are springing up and selling for less. 
 After retiring as chief executive of TRW, Joseph T. Gorman put his house in Shaker Heights, which has seven bedrooms and is more than 13,000 square feet, up for sale. He first asked $2.35 million. It was on the market for two years, and in July finally sold for $1,665,000. So far this year, it is the only big sale in Shaker Heights, even though five properties priced at more than $1 million are on the market.
 ''It's very unusual for Shaker Heights to have five houses on the market in this price range,'' said Monique Plociak, a real estate agent with Realty One. According to Ms. Plociak, several years ago, homes in the very high-end market sold more quickly, keeping the inventory low. Some houses changed hands without ever being listed.
 In other parts of the country, a million-plus home might seem like a bargain. But in Greater Cleveland, where the average price for a single-family home is $171,012, finding buyers in the $2 million price range has been challenging lately not just in Shaker Heights, but in Greater Cleveland, as companies like TRW have moved their headquarters out of the Cleveland area.
 On South Park Boulevard, the most exclusive street in Shaker Heights, Ms. Plociak is currently listing a 7,686-square-foot colonial once owned by Art Modell, former owner of the Cleveland Browns and the Baltimore Ravens football teams. The house, which was initially listed for $3.3 million in January but has since come down to $2.75 million, is situated on an acre and a half of beautifully landscaped real estate. Original craftsmanship is still intact throughout the house, such as ornately carved oak paneling, stone walls and an iron balcony in the octagonal foyer. Mr. Modell added a great room for entertaining football players, but aside from this modification, the structure of the house remains as it was built in 1937.
 And that, it seems, is part of the problem. The house has only a two-car garage, the laundry is in the basement, and there is no master bedroom on the first floor, or high-tech wiring. Those aspects limit the appeal of the house to a small subset of buyers who like old houses and can afford to pay $70,000 a year in taxes. The current owners, who are in their 70's, have lived here for 30 years. Much of the decor is traditional, with peach-colored wall-to-wall carpeting covering hardwood floors and heavy drapes obscuring original leaded-glass windows.
 ''Five years ago, this house would have sold in a day,'' Ms. Plociak said. While the property tax rate in Shaker Heights remains the same as it was five years ago, fewer people seem to be willing to absorb the cost as other options become available. There is more competition now, she said, with new houses not much farther away that are less expensive, with lower taxes. ''They're easier to maintain and they have all the bells and whistles,'' she said. ''And you don't have to come in and redecorate.''
 Typical of the competition she is talking about is the Barrington Estate Homes and Golf Club Community, a gated Jack Nicklaus Signature golf course community about 30 miles from downtown Cleveland. A new home features natural materials like shell-stone tile flooring, hand-carved molding and custom paint in earth tones. Fiber-optic wiring is capable of handling 36 telephone lines and supports an elaborate security system and zoned lighting, while the latest insulation and double-paned windows are energy efficient.
 Perhaps more important than high-tech amenities and neutral decor is the allocation of space. The 7,676-square-foot house has a four-car garage, first-floor master suite, a media room, multiple computer stations and a climate-controlled wine cellar. It also features one of the latest trends in new home building, a ''hearth room,'' which is essentially an enlarged kitchen with a fireplace.
 She has listed the house at $1.8 million and hopes to close a deal with a player for the Cleveland Browns in the very near future. Property taxes on this house are $24,000, about half of her Shaker listing, both because of the lower-selling price, but also because outlying communities do not have the same school costs. 
 Real estate agents and market analysts agree that the very high-end market is struggling throughout Greater Cleveland. But with virtually no population growth in the region, competition for buyers is increasing as large new houses are built on the fringes and ever-expanding highways make longer commutes easier. 
 But not everyone looking for high-tech amenities wants to be in a distant suburb, or live in a new house. To that end, Bradford Remington, owner of Main Street Holdings, a renovation company that specializes in older homes, is working on a house on South Park in Shaker Heights built in 1919 that he bought for $1.05 million. While leaving much of the original architectural detailing in place, he is installing air-conditioning and new wiring, and is reconfiguring the ground floor space. He plans to put about $600,000 into it and to sell it for $2 million.
 Thomas E. Bier, director of the Center for Housing Research and Policy at the Maxine Goodman Levine College of Urban Affairs at Cleveland State University, agrees that updating older homes in the high-end market could prove to be a good business. While he has not broken down figures specifically for this price range, his research concludes that homes built in the 20's and 30's have maintained their value over time. 
 Louis Tisler, executive director of the First Suburbs Consortium Development Council, a nonprofit group advocating on behalf of older, inner-ring suburbs in Greater Cleveland, called these homes ''great assets in this community.''
 ''With new construction you can get more house, but are you getting more home?'' he said. ''If you're looking at a South Park address and putting money into it, that is a good investment.''

But newer houses at Barrington Estate Homes, below, have vast kitchens and desirable modern features. (Photographs by David Maxwell for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Housing for Artists? How About Bicycle Repairmen?

BYLINE: By JOE QUEENAN. 
Joe Queenan is the author of ''True Believers: The Tragic Inner Life of Sports Fans,'' released in paperback by Picador USA.

 BODY:
I LIKE artists as much as the next guy, but when I read that a Jersey City developer was converting a warehouse into an apartment building that would reserve six units for low-and moderate-income artists, I could feel my blood boil. According to news reports, Jersey City is already home to 1,600 artists, so it's not like six more Andy Warhol clones is going to make that much of a difference. Moreover, Jersey City is right across the river from New York, home to tens of thousands of artists, so the idea of designating special housing for even more painters and sculptors, whatever their economic condition, seems like a waste of precious resources.
 Why is it always artists who get the cheap apartments and converted lofts? Mainly because real estate developers are awarded tax breaks and variances if they agree to set aside a few units for those of a creative bent. But is this fair to taxpayers? Or to young people in less glamorous walks of life? Why can't these burgeoning burgs give tax breaks to developers who earmark housing for young people who are not artists? 
 Who do I have in mind? Certified financial planners, for starters. This is one of the most important occupations in America today, yet the average person erroneously believes that most certified financial planners are already wealthy. Not true. It takes years and years to establish oneself in this socially munificent profession, and young, lower-income C.F.P.'s have just as a strong a claim on society's resources as anyone else. Moreover, financial planners are less noisy than artists, much tidier, more civic-minded and more likely to provide products and services that society needs.
 I am certainly not suggesting than communities like San Francisco, Santa Fe and New York should stop subsidizing the arts, or that they should start diverting all of their resources to housing for financial planners. Nor am I arguing that C.F.P.'s are the only young people who should benefit from subsidized housing. Other occupations immediately come to mind. 
 For example, when was the last time that any major American city announced that it would set aside housing for cash-strapped bicycle repairmen? By keeping our bicycles in tip-top shape, these admirable youngsters make the roads safer and help ease urban congestion. Unlike many artists, they are not introverted, antisocial and mopey; I personally would love to live in a neighborhood filled with aspiring bicycle repairmen. But, lacking subsidized housing, none of them can afford to live here.
 Are there any other occupations that could benefit from subsidized housing? You bet. Proofreaders, volleyball coaches, baristas and short-order cooks all play a vital role in this society, yet we never hear of any developer setting aside apartments for them. Laundromat managers, tanning spa personnel and physical therapists are all vital cogs in the wheel of democracy, yet where are the handouts for them? 
 Another thing that bothers me about subsidized housing for artists is that painters, sculptors and producers of annoying video installations always take precedence over musicians and writers. Satire is one of the toughest rackets to break into in these United States, yet I have never once heard of any municipality converting old warehouses into public housing for wits, pundits or gadflies, much less curmudgeons. French horn players and bassoonists regularly have to pawn their Mozart scores to pay their heating bills, but nobody ever talks about subsidized housing for them. And don't even get me started on the economic misfortunes of the modern urban poet.
 The time has come for cities all across this great nation to rethink subsidy policies. America already has enough artists; what it really needs are more dental hygienists, more income-tax preparers, more teaching assistants. If Jersey City is serious about improving its quality of life, it should tell developers to set aside apartments for fledgling dog walkers, trainee barbers, neophyte bus drivers and cobbler's apprentices. The difference between 1,600 artists and 1,606 artists is minuscule; but bringing in six bright young pension-fund managers could really make Jersey City hop!
 Still, in doing this civic leaders must maintain standards. For example, if I ever read that developers are building low-income housing for stand-up comics, mimes, Civil War re-enactors or folk singers who butcher ''Blowin' in the Wind'' on the subway, I'm leaving the country for good. 
 And frankly, I don't think I'll be the only one leaving.

HEADLINE: The 2004 Republican National Convention

BYLINE: By WILLIAM McNULTY, JAMES BRONZAN and JOE BURGESS

 BODY:

Planned Protests
Disruptions can be expected along the protest routes during the convention.

SUNDAY

1. Say No to the Bush Agenda, United for Peace and Justice
A national coalition of nearly 900 groups is planning a march at noon from north of 14th St. at Seventh Ave. past the convention site to Union Square Park. Organizers say it could attract 250,000 people.

MONDAY

2. March and Rally for Justice
Sponsored by Still We Rise, a coalition of more than 50 nonprofit organizations, many of them New York-based groups serving people infected with H.I.V., immigrants, the poor and the homeless. The demonstration begins at noon at Union Square and proceeds up Eighth Avenue to Madison Square Garden.

TUESDAY

Direct Action Day
An anarchist collective calling itself the A31 Action Coalition has organized a series of nonviolent civil disobedience events that will use parts of Midtown to stage demonstrations, without permits.

WEDNESDAY

3. Worker's Rally
At 4 p.m., the New York City Central Labor Council of the A.F.L.-C.I.O., which represents nearly 400 New York City unions, is holding a rally on Eighth Avenue between 23rd and 30th Streets.

4. Women's Rally
At 7 p.m., the National Organization for Women has planned what could become the largest rally in Central Park during the convention. They anticipate up to 50,000 people will gather on the East Meadow to draw women's issues.

5. The Line
From 8:13 to 8:31 a.m., in a demonstration organized by People for the American Way; the Imagine Festival of Arts, Issues and Ideas; and the New York State A.F.L.-C.I.O., thousands of people will form a symbolic unemployment line from Wall Street to Madison Square Garden.

Avoiding Penn Station
Long Island Rail Road commuters may wish to transfer to the subway at station stops in Brooklyn and Queens as an alternative to traveling into Penn Station.

Transfer to the 7 train, from the L.I.R.R. to Midtown Manhattan and Times Square can be made at the Woodside, Hunterspoint Ave. and Long Island City stations. Hunterspoint and Long Island City stations are rush-hour service only.

Convenient connections to subway lines serving Lower and Midtown Manhattan can be made at the L.I.R.R. Flatbush Ave. Terminal. Transfer can be made to the 2, 3, 4, 5, B, D, M, N, R and Q trains.

The L.I.R.R. Jamaica Hub offers connections to the J, Z and E trains. Also AirTrain service to Kennedy Airport.

At the Garden
Two entrances to Penn Station will remain open.
