HEADLINE: Coming Soon: The Vanishing Work Force

BYLINE: By EDUARDO PORTER

DATELINE: PITTSBURGH 

 BODY:
TO be perfectly blunt about it, Pittsburgh is getting old. Half the line workers who repair, maintain and upgrade the grid at Duquesne Light, its electric utility, will be eligible to retire by the end of the decade. Likewise, half the 6,500 nurses working at the University of Pittsburgh Medical Center hospitals will hit the typical retirement age of 55 in the next seven years. And just outside of town, at Westinghouse Electric, which designs and maintains nuclear power generators, the average age of engineers is the late 40's. 
 The trend has some people worried. ''A silent crisis threatens the prosperity of Pittsburgh and western Pennsylvania,'' warned a report done two years ago by the Center for Competitive Workforce Development at Duquesne University. ''A declining and aging population places at risk the stability of the region's work force and opportunities for economic progress.''
 In other words, there just may not be enough young workers to go around in the not-too-distant future. So the older ones may have to stick around a lot longer than they originally planned. 
 That may seem counterintuitive, given the hand-wringing across the country about the sluggish pace of job creation. Indeed, Pittsburgh is flush with surplus workers, judging from an unemployment rate that jumped to 5.7 percent in July from 4.2 percent three years ago. The city's power industry, in particular, has shed about 40 percent of its jobs in the last 10 years. 
 Yet a peek through the demographic keyhole presents a startlingly different picture. More than 16 percent of Pittsburgh's population is above the official retirement age of 65. By 2012, southwestern Pennsylvania could face a shortage of 125,000 workers -- about a tenth the size of today's labor force, according to the Duquesne University report. 
 And what is happening in Pittsburgh appears to be an early installment of a demographic drama unfolding across the nation. As workers age, fewer new bodies are coming up the pipeline to replace them. According to projections from the Bureau of Labor Statistics, the working-age population -- adults 16 to 54 who are neither in the military nor in jail -- will have grown by six million people from 2002 to 2012. By contrast, the 55-and-over age bracket will have expanded by 18 million.
 The shift will only intensify in the years that follow. By 2030, people 55 and older will make up 37 percent of the adult population, up from 15 percent today. ''This is a sleeper issue,'' said Charles E.M. Kolb, president of the Committee for Economic Development, a group of business and education leaders that conducts policy research in Washington. ''We do have a demographic time bomb.''
 How will it all play out? The wait at McDonald's may be a lot longer: the National Restaurant Association projects a shortage of 1.5 million restaurant workers by 2014. Or maybe the mail won't be delivered as fast: more than a quarter of all postal workers who were employed in 1998 will have retired by the end of 2008, according to a bureau study. Even the local house of worship may become a little crowded: one in five members of the clergy will be of pension age over the same period. Over all, 13 percent more people are expected to retire from 2003 to 2008 than did so in the previous five years. 
 Some companies -- though obviously not a majority -- are taking the longer view, by scrambling to secure tomorrow's work force. In 2002, Duquesne Light set up a two-year program at Allegheny County Community College to train new line workers. 
 The University of Pittsburgh Medical Center is trying to recruit new nurses and retain veterans with a new career track that would allow them to continue to advance and receive pay increases. ''Most nurses leave clinical practice at about 55,'' said Gail Wolf, the chief nursing officer. ''We need to keep them at the bedside longer.''
 OTHER companies are turning to sources farther afield. The restaurant industry, for instance, is lobbying for easier immigration restrictions. Many companies, of course, are simply outsourcing jobs -- shifting production to labor-rich countries abroad. 
 But experts say that these efforts will be of little consequence, given the magnitude of the coming demographic dislocation. Like it or not, to deal with the aging of America's labor force, workers will probably be made to work longer. 
 ''Many businesses feel they have found solutions by sending jobs offshore or bringing immigrants to fill positions here,'' said Larry Anderson, president of the National Older Worker Career Center in Arlington, Va. ''But the problem is much larger. Business is going to have to tap the older work force.''
 On Friday, Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, suggested to economists meeting in Jackson Hole, Wyo., that Social Security and Medicare benefits should be curtailed to avoid fiscal disaster and keep workers on the job longer.
 Today, a 65-year-old is expected to live to 83. That is almost three years longer than the life expectancy of a 65-year-old in 1970. But the aging of the work force is not just about longer life spans. It has more to do with a steep decline in the nation's production of young people -- a birth dearth. 
 The labor force started to swell in the second half of the 1960's, when the baby-boom generation began to come of age. But the boom was followed by a baby bust. The fertility rate dropped from 3.5 children per woman in the mid-1950's to about 2 in the 1970's. As a result, when the boomers start exiting the work force -- as they will in droves starting around 2008 -- they will leave a big demographic hole behind them.
 Aging is not just an American issue. According to projections from the United Nations, the median age in Switzerland is 40, five years older than that of the United States. And by the time America's median age reaches 40, around midcentury, half of all Italians will be over 52. 
 The United States has staved off the national aging process mostly by drawing immigrants -- who, on average, are younger and have higher fertility rates than the general American population. A study by the Center for Labor Market Studies at Northeastern University in Boston found that new immigrants accounted for 47 percent of the increase in the labor force from 1990 to 2000. 
 Yet the poor countries that are sending their workers to the United States are getting older, too -- some at an even faster rate. Today, half of all Mexicans are under 25. But by 2050, Mexico's median age is expected to rise to 42, according to United Nations figures. 
 Aging, meanwhile, will steadily worsen America's labor outlook. The participation rate of the general population in the work force climbed to 67.2 percent in 2000 from 59.2 percent in 1950. But projections from the Labor Department call for it to fall back to 62.3 percent by 2030.
 As a result, the expansion of the labor force will slow, to just 0.6 percent a year over the first half of the 21st century, from 1.6 percent in the second half of the 20th. And the economy's dependency ratio, which measures the burden of retirees on workers, will narrow. In 2000, there were five people aged 20 to 64 for each person 65 or older. By 2030, the ratio will be less than 3 to 1. 
 Not everyone is convinced that a graying labor force will be a problem. Peter Cappelli, an economist at the Wharton School of the University of Pennsylvania, contends that unemployment leaves a big pool of workers that companies can tap even as aging reduces the overall size of the labor force. 
 He noted that the abundance of baby-boomer labor, though a boon for employers, wasn't so great for workers. Indeed, average hourly earnings of production workers in private industry fell by more than 15 percent, adjusted for inflation, as the boomers flowed through the workplace from the early 1970's to the mid-1990's. 
 If labor markets tighten, Mr. Cappelli argued, wages will rise and productivity will accelerate, sustaining economic growth. Moreover, higher wages may increase the supply of workers by drawing older people back into the job market. 
 But many experts are less sanguine. A 1998 study by economists at the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development in Paris found that the rise in the dependency ratio alone could shrink United States living standards, as measured by per capita income, by about 10 percent by 2050. 
 It is also far from clear that Americans want to work longer. An AARP survey last year of workers over 50 found that 68 percent planned to work after the usual retirement age, but a Harris survey in 1992 among people who had already retired found that nearly 86 percent either were not able to work or simply did not want to. 
 ''Our older people are staying on longer because they can't afford to retire,'' said Ronald M. Moquist, president and chief executive of Raven Industries in Sioux Falls, S.D., a plastics and electronics maker.
 Over the past 50 years, both corporate and federal policies have encouraged workers to leave the rat race and to go bask in Florida as early as possible. Social Security payments, for example, were structured in 1935 to favor retirement as soon as 62 and no later than 65. After that, the extra benefits earned from working one more year did not make up for the year's delay in getting the benefits. Companies also built their pension plans to favor early retirement and make way for younger workers. 
 So it is perhaps not surprising that until recently, workers were retiring sooner rather than later. In 1950, some 87 percent of men aged 55 to 64 and 46 percent of those over 65 were working. By 2000, the shares had dropped, to 67 percent for the younger group and 17 percent for the older one. 
 One way or another, companies may need to figure out ways to entice workers to toil later in life. Rudolph G. Penner, an expert on aging at the Urban Institute, a research organization in Washington, says he believes that employers should make it easier for older workers to stay on the job, through flexible work schedules and phased retirement that allows them to reduce their workload gradually before full retirement.
 The government may have to play a part, too. Because higher health care costs are a big disincentive to keeping older workers, Mr. Penner said the government should offer Medicare as primary insurance, rather than secondary, to the elderly employed. That would make it cheaper for employers to keep them. And the government should readjust rules to allow employers to offer older workers sliding scales of benefits for part-time or occasional work. 
 Indeed, some of these forces are already aligning. Many traditional company pension plans, for example, are being replaced by defined-contribution plans like 401(k)'s. That provides an incentive for employees who have lost the security of their defined-benefit pensions to work longer and build their nest eggs.
 And the ballooning cost of health insurance cuts both ways. Though rising premiums are rough on employers, they also make it harder for people to leave their jobs -- and their employer-provided health coverage -- before Medicare kicks in at 65.
 THE effort to shore up Social Security -- to compensate for more and longer-lived retirees -- has prompted the government to start gradually raising the age when a worker can claim full benefits, to 67 from 65. But some experts say more is needed. In 1999, the Committee for Economic Development recommended that the full retirement age for Social Security payments be raised gradually to 70 over three decades -- and then indexed to longevity. 
 All of this is sapping workers' confidence in their ability to retire. According to the 2004 Retirement Confidence Survey, an annual study done by two Washington organizations, the Employers Benefit Research Institute and the American Savings Education Council, barely 36 percent of workers are confident that they will have enough money to take care of basic expenses during retirement. And the share of workers who expect to retire before 65 has dropped to 37 percent from 49 percent a decade ago.
 ''The good news is that we are living longer,'' said Mr. Kolb, the Committee for Economic Development president. ''But people are finding they haven't saved enough to afford the lifestyle they want, so by necessity people are staying longer in the work force.''
 The American economy may need them soon.

HEADLINE: Old, in the Way And Hard at Work

BYLINE: By EDUARDO PORTER

 BODY:
THE good news is we are living longer than ever before. The bad news is it's going to cost us. 
 As global fertility rates grow more slowly and increasing prosperity enhances life expectancy, a complicated side effect has emerged: big chunks of the world are starting to look like geriatric wards. It is uncertain how the world will pay for them. 
 A larger population of retirees, living longer, mixed with fewer young people means that the labor force will shrink as a percentage of the total population. With fewer people at work to support everyone else, living standards could fall. 
 Aging will put stress on government finances, drawing more and more money from budgets to pay for pensions and health care. Interest rates could rise sharply as a result, with the old drawing down their lifelong savings, depressing savings rates across the industrial world. 
 The upshot is that the bulging cohort of the elderly will probably be forced to work well into what today is considered old age, and then get stingier pensions when they eventually retire. Moreover, their children will have to work harder to support them. 
 ''This is the result of desirable trends,'' says Joseph Chamie, director of the United Nations population division. ''Lower mortality is very good news. But we've got to adjust the system.''
 Consider the situation in Japan. With half the population over the age 41, Japan is among the oldest countries. With one of the lowest fertility rates -- 1.32 children per woman -- by 2050 there will be about 110 million Japanese, down from 127 million in 2000. And half of them will be over 53.
 According to projections by economists at the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development, Japanese public spending on pensions and health will increase by some 3 percent of the gross domestic product by 2050. (The total American budget deficit, which is considered to be too large, is 3.5 percent of the gross domestic product.)
 Aging will also whittle away Japanese prosperity by cutting into the work force. In 2000 there were almost three people of working age for every person over 60. According to United Nations projections, by 2050 there will be less than one. By 2050, Japan's per capita income would be 23 percent lower than it would be if the dependency ratio remained stable, according to the Organization of Economic Cooperation and Development study. 
 This profile is far from unique. By 2050, half of all Italians and Spaniards, for example, will be over 52 years old.
 Even in the United States, the youngest of the wealthy nations, those over 65 are expected to represent 19 percent of the population in 2030, from 12 percent in 2000, when the bulge of baby boomers reach the autumn of their lives. Even by midcentury, when most boomers will be dead, 21 percent of the population will be over 65. 
 The poor world might help ease the rich world's plight. Broadly speaking, higher fertility rates and lower life expectancies mean that poorer nations are much younger than rich ones. In 2000, the median age in India was 23. Half the population of Brazil was under 25. According to United Nations estimates, virtually all of the increase in the world's population -- from 6.1 billion in 2000 to 8.9 billion in 2050-- will come from the so-called developing world. And its share of the global economy will rise to more than half from about a third today.
 Developed countries are relying on some of this youth by investing in factories in poorer nations and drawing workers by easing immigration restrictions.
 Yet such steps are likely to be only temporary palliatives, because countries with the pool of workers are getting old, too. The median age of China's population in 2000 was 30, five years younger than that of the United States. By 2050 it will be 43.8, four years older than America's. And in India, half the population will be over 38.
 The countries that will most suffer from aging populations are in the former Soviet bloc. Today, the 10 nations with the lowest rates of population growth are, in order, Estonia, Latvia, Georgia, Bulgaria, Ukraine, Lithuania, Russia, Hungary, Armenia and Belarus. Estonia's population will halve by 2050, to 657,000, and 42 percent of those left will be more than 60 years old.
 So what is the world to do? As more people live into their 80's and 90's, governments across the developed world will have to keep workers in the labor force longer and avoid a swell of budgetary red ink by revamping pension systems; retirement will be pushed back and benefits will be less generous.
 In the last several years, the Japanese government increased workers' pension contributions, raised the age at which pensions could be drawn to 65 from 60, and cut pension benefits for future retirees. The government is also offering grants to companies that hire older workers.
 In the United States, business leaders and some government officials have called for raising the retirement age. The full retirement age under Social Security is already being gradually increased to 67, from 65. In testimony before Congress on Friday, Federal Reserve chairman Alan Greenspan, 78, repeated his call for an increase. 
 Governments around the world are trying all sorts of incentives to encourage families to have more children. Several European governments offer tax reductions for each additional child. And last year, Rocco Falivena, the mayor of Laviano, near Naples in southern Italy, offered women 10,000 euros (about $12,000 today) for each child they bear. 
 Unsurprisingly, many of these initiatives are meeting stiff resistance, bound as they are to cause scuffles between young and old. Already, in Japan and China offspring are legally bound to care for their parents. 
 Demands by the elderly for government services might draw public financing away from education and other priorities of the young. Sociologists in Europe have discussed the merits of giving voting rights to children, exercised by their parents, to protect the young from gerontocratic power. 
 ''We've gone from Pampers to Depends,'' says Mr. Chamie of the United Nations.
 The political battles of the future will be about who pays for them.

HEADLINE: A Greener Globe, Maybe

BYLINE: By ANDREW C. REVKIN

 BODY:
WITH all the hand-wringing about the economic perils of falling populations, and the prospect of a spreading demographic shift from explosion to implosion, there could be one beneficiary: the environment. 
 For decades, the rise in human numbers has been seen as the chief force threatening rain forests, depleting fisheries, choking the air and polluting the waters. So would an end to humanity's growth spurt make possible a not-too-diminished world with enough room for some wild things, with reasonably breathable air and drinkable water, with a livable climate?
 Many demographers, economists and ecologists are guardedly, often very guardedly, optimistic. 
 On the plus side, it is becoming clear that the heedless polluting that accompanied 20th-century industrialization is unlikely to be repeated by today's industrializing countries. Globalization, often portrayed as an environmental villain, may help, some experts say, because multinational corporations, setting up factories in poor countries, tend to set higher environmental standards, prompting local communities to demand similar standards for home-grown industries. China, far and away the most important of the developing economies, is already pushing to reduce sooty emissions. 
 Increasing urbanization should also help the environment. Almost all of the extra three billion or so people expected by midcentury will live in or around cities, according to studies by the United Nations and the National Academy of Sciences. City dwellers tend to use energy and other resources more efficiently, and have less direct impact on untrammeled landscapes like forests. 
 Much depends, however, on how those urbanites live, and think. ''An end to growing population pressure in rural areas could be good for protection of the environment,'' said Dr. Joel E. Cohen, the director of the Laboratory of Populations of Columbia and Rockefeller Universities. ''But only if the people who live in the cities understand that it's of interest to preserve watersheds, agricultural lands and wildlife areas.'' 
 On the minus side, some benefits of urbanization could be offset by a drop in the number of people per household. That means more households, more urban and suburban sprawl, and less efficient use of resources. Last year, Jianguo R. Liu at Michigan State, Paul R.Ehrlich at Stanford and other experts estimated in the journal Nature that by 2015, biological ''hot spots'' -- regions where human activity threatens habitat rich in rare species, as in South Florida and China's panda preserves -- would have 233 million extra households because of declining numbers of people per dwelling. 
 Also on the minus side: Even if the human population begins to decline after 2050, global warming, which is caused by the buildup in the atmosphere of so-called greenhouse gases like carbon dioxide, is projected to cause major disruptions in the environment. Recent computer simulations, based on the current growth in energy use, estimate a 5 degree rise in the earth's average temperature in this century. 
 Without reduced use of current fuels, according to many scientists, the world population at midcentury -- 50 percent larger than today's but requiring three times as much energy -- will not be able to avert melting polar ice, shifting drought and flood patterns, and sea levels possibly rising a yard or more. 
 The environmental future depends on human behavior, not just human numbers. Clearly, it will be far easier to accommodate nine billion people than the 12 billion once anticipated. But not, many experts say, if all of them consume materials, energy and other goods at the rate Americans do now. 
 Some projections, however, offer some reason for optimism. In 2002, Paul E. Waggoner of the Connecticut Agricultural Experiment Station and Jesse H. Ausubel at Rockefeller University calculated that a 2 to 3 percent annual improvement in some combination of the efficiency of technologies used to make things or a decline in the waste produced in consuming things could offset the environmental damage from having 50 percent more people 50 years from now. 
 But in the end, some scientists argue, humanity cannot sustain the quality of the environment until it thinks of itself as part of, not separate from, the natural world.
 ''That may seem like pie in the sky,'' says Daniel B. Botkin, an ecologist and author of ''Discordant Harmonies: A New Ecology for the 21st Century,'' but in the crowded, urbanizing world to come, he concludes, such an understanding will be essential.

HEADLINE: From Deep in the Depression, Three Low-Rise Corners

BYLINE: By CHRISTOPHER GRAY

 BODY:
''During the boom, the sound of the riveter was loud in the city. Now it is so quiet you can hear a girder fall for miles away.'' 
 SO wrote James Thurber in January 1932 in his article ''New York in the Third Winter'' in Fortune magazine, as the last echoes of the building boom of the 1920's faded away.
 In place of new Park Avenue duplexes, big Midtown hotels and flashy Wall Street skyscrapers came two-story taxpayers, small buildings meant as temporary measures whose rental income would help cover the tax bill until economic times improved. 
 A good number of these low-rise monuments to expediency have survived for more than half a century -- a fact that is hard to believe, especially since they exist in a continually expanding real estate market. 
 Taxpayers, with their own distinct signatures, became the prototypical architectural development of the Depression decade. They were often built on land owned by an estate or controlled by a chain store -- those owners had the deep pockets necessary for reconstruction. 
 They were not just two-story versions of 1920's architecture, but emphasized new materials and forms, usually in the Art Deco or Art Moderne style.
 They were all dependent on retail traffic -- even in a Depression, New Yorkers bought newspapers, smoked and chewed gum -- and so such stores usually occupied corner sites. On Madison, Lexington and Third Avenues, 7 percent of the building plans filed in 1929 were for one- to three-story buildings; by 1933, it was 100 percent. 
 These new low-rises were the work of a new generation of architects, whose names are generally absent from the guidebooks to New York's important buildings -- names like Arthur Weiser, William I. Hohauser and J. M. Berlinger. 
 The big designers who controlled the profession in the 1920's were humbled: Emery Roth, who had done the San Remo at 74th and Central Park West, the Beresford at 81st and Central Park West and the Ritz Tower at 57th and Park, entered a 1933 competition for a two-story building in Times Square, and even that was not built. Roth got second place and a check for $150. 
 In 1935, at the southeast corner of 79th and Lexington, Hohauser designed a $12,000 taxpayer for the Liggett's drugstore chain, replacing a brownstone row house built on a 1,200-square-foot plot long before Lexington was a commercial street.
 Hohauser gave it a facade of green, tan and gun-metal porcelain enamel panels surrounding industrial-style windows, with a Moderne-style chevron over the doorway to the second floor. 
 Although now obscured by later canvas awnings, it's a perfect little jewel of the type, and the notoriously demanding New Yorker critic Lewis Mumford considered it ''quite decent.'' 
 On the West Side, in 1938 Weiser designed a two-story building on the southeast corner of 72nd and Broadway, replacing another corner row house.
 For $25,000, Weiser erected a crisp, shoebox-shaped structure, on a 1,500-square-foot lot, and covered it with large, square concave panels in a cream color. 
 A tiny strip of bright polychromed terra cotta running along the top was the structure's only decoration -- if you discount the 80-foot-long sign panel set into the second floor that advertised ''National Beauty Parlors.''
 This building, too, has been swamped by signage, particularly the huge Sleepy's sign at the second floor. 
 In 1939, Berlinger, who worked extensively in tenement alteration, designed one of New York's most unusual taxpayers, the little modernist corner building at John Street and Broadway. The Broadway section is two stories high, the part farther down John Street six stories tall, all on an irregular plot of about 4,500 square feet. 
 A subway station is underneath the Broadway side of the building, and perhaps it was for this reason that Berlinger erected the lower portion at Broadway, because of limits in foundation size. 
 The materials were strictly vernacular Art Deco -- tan ceramic panels and black glass piers. But the geometry of the two sections -- one low, with a curved corner, the other high, with spare, rectangular forms -- was quite European. 
 The result was a striking work for New York that emulated German modernist design, like the Fagus factory in Alfeld an der Leine, Germany, by Walter Gropius.
 A particularly nice fillip is the separate fire staircase on the high portion -- in photographs it looks as if it is encased in glass, but it is actually a simple screen of wire mesh. 
 Taxpayers are really just shards of buildings, and many have in fact been swept up in two decades of rising land values.
 A limestone one-story taxpayer designed in 1923 at 96th and Madison on a 3,500-square-foot lot was demolished earlier this year for a 13-story apartment house.
 But not every low building is developable, especially when existing rents and billboard revenues sweeten the low-rise pot.
 At 79th and Lexington, John Bono, head of Headington Wines and Liquors, said that his father started the business in 1941 and that he now owns the building. ''Almost every day, we have offers,'' he said, ''but they are people who want to combine it with other parcels'' and don't have them yet. He added, ''I don't think development would work here anytime soon -- this is a very prosperous retail location.'' 
 At Broadway and John Street, Richard Marcus, the property manager, said that one family had owned the building for nearly half a century. 
 He also says that others, including himself, have tried to combine the plot with a larger assemblage, but without success, and that the site itself is a bit small, especially with the construction concerns posed by the subway complex underneath. 
 Over at 72nd and Broadway, the owner of the property is registered as East End Associates, which is apparently part of the estate of the late Stanley Stahl, a major property investor. 
 Marianne Dziuba, senior vice president of Stahl Real Estate, did not return repeated telephone calls.
 The Upper West Side/Central Park West Historic District, designated in 1990, was drawn to include this modest work. So even though it is now nearly invisible under advertising signs, Weiser's building is likely to survive for quite some time.

LENGTH: 1306 words

HEADLINE: Delegates Leaning to Right Of G.O.P. and the Nation

BYLINE: By KATHARINE Q. SEELYE and MARJORIE CONNELLY

 BODY:
THE Republican Party intends to show a somewhat moderate face to the nation during the convention this week in Madison Square Garden. But the delegates who will gather here and are shaping the party platform hold more conservative views than do most Republican voters, and substantially more conservative ones than those of voters at large, according to a New York Times/CBS News Poll.
 The delegates are nearly unanimous in their support for the war in Iraq. A full 96 percent said the United States did the right thing going into Iraq, compared with only 78 percent of all voters who call themselves Republicans and 46 percent of all voters. The figures on voters came from a separate, nationwide Times/CBS News Poll.
 And 86 percent of the delegates said the results so far in Iraq were worth the loss of American lives, compared with 70 percent of Republican voters and 36 percent of all voters. 
 The poll of 1,200 of the 2,509 delegates was conducted from Aug. 3 to Aug. 23 by telephone, fax and e-mail. It has a margin of sampling error of plus or minus two percentage points. The nationwide poll of 823 voters was conducted by telephone from July 11 to July 15, and has a margin of sampling error of plus or minus three percentage points.
 Two-thirds of the delegates described themselves as conservative, while one-third said they were moderate. Almost none of them perceive President Bush as a liberal, although 8 percent of Republican voters do.
 On social issues, the delegates tend to reflect their party. For example, 38 percent of both delegates and Republican voters say abortion should be outlawed altogether. Similarly, they reflect the party's division over embryonic stem cell research.
 But they are more conservative than the party as a whole when it comes to gay marriage. Just 3 percent of delegates say gay couples should be allowed to marry, while 11 percent of Republican voters would allow it. Almost half the delegates, about the same amount of Republican voters, said there should be no legal recognition of gay marriage, although 41 percent of delegates and 36 percent of all Republicans said gay couples should be allowed to form civil unions.
 Delegates generally take a more unilateral stance on foreign policy, believe in a more limited role for government and believe more in free trade than their fellow Republicans or voters at large. And they are happier with the current level of legal immigration than other Republicans.
 Two-thirds of the delegates said the United States should be able to attack any country it thinks might attack the United States, while only half of Republican voters and a third of all voters agreed.
 And of the three groups, delegates have the least regard for the United Nations. Only 7 percent said it was extremely important for the United States to work through the United Nations to solve international problems, while a third of all Republicans and half of all voters agree.
 Just 3 percent of the delegates said that the United States should have stayed out of Iraq, while 19 percent of Republican voters and half of all voters agreed with that statement.
 The delegates are more in sync with their party on the question of whether the United States should try to change a dictatorship to a democracy where it can. About 40 percent of the delegates and Republican voters agree with that idea, while only 15 percent of Democratic voters do.
 The delegates named the economy as the most important issue in their states, but they also rated terrorism and defense as the next most important issues.
 In a follow-up interview, Norman Riffel, 65, a delegate from Springfield, Neb., who owns a moving and storage company, said: ''President Bush has to lay out a plan for addressing terrorism, creating more jobs and building stronger relationships with other nations. These are all high priorities.''
 In something of a rebuff to Republican officials who picked New York for the convention as an evocation of what they perceive as President Bush's finest hour, nearly one in five delegates said they should have picked someplace else.
 Thirty-one percent said they were somewhat or very worried about a terrorist attack in New York, slightly more than the 27 percent of Democratic delegates who were worried about an attack in Boston. But New Yorkers are even more concerned than the delegates, with 53 percent of them worried about a terrorist attack this week.
 While 94 percent of the Republican delegates said they had a good impression of New York, they expressed little interest in venturing out into the city. Asked if there was any particular place or thing that they wanted to be sure to see while here, the biggest percentage (29) said ''nothing.'' The next-highest percentage (21) named the Statue of Liberty, and the third-highest (16) said ground zero.
 They hold Rudolph W. Giuliani, who was mayor during the terrorist attacks, in high regard, even though he is more socially liberal than many of them. Ninety-one percent said they had a favorable view of Mr. Giuliani, which is nearly as many as the 94 percent with a favorable view of Vice President Dick Cheney.
 They take a dimmer view of Senator John McCain, the maverick Arizona Republican, who was rated favorably by 62 percent (but who often tops other polls as one of the most popular politicians in America).
 In interviews, the delegates applauded President Bush, citing his character rather than specific policies. ''His conviction is his greatest strength,'' said Paul Willis, 50, a delegate from Pahrump, Nev., and a retired carpenter.
 But like others, Marti Halverson, a homemaker from Etna, Wyo., expressed concern that the president did not always put his best foot forward. ''His greatest weakness is inability to communicate,'' she said. 
 She also said he had a job to do at the convention. 
 ''He has to convince the American public that in this case, in the case of Iraq, might is right,'' she said. ''Because so many good Republicans are doubting the plan.''
 People who become delegates to their party's conventions tend to be political activists, many of them driven into politics because of firm views on an issue or philosophy. Delegates to both the Republican and Democratic conventions in recent years have tended to be farther to the right or the left than voters in their own party and certainly farther from the center than the public at large.
 The Republican delegates are adamantly in favor of free trade, even at the cost of jobs at home, and strongly against regulation of business and the environment. This is substantially at odds with Republican voters as a whole.
 And more than their fellow Republicans, the delegates said they preferred cutting taxes to reducing the deficit, were less concerned that antiterrorism measures were restricting civil liberties, and believed that presidential candidates should discuss the role of religion in their private lives as part of their campaigns.
 The Republican delegates are less diverse by race, gender and income than the delegates at the Democratic convention were, but they are more diverse than the delegates at previous Republican conventions.
 For example, 85 percent of the delegates at this convention are white, compared with 89 percent in 2000 and 91 percent in 1996. Six percent are black, compared with 2 percent in 2000. (In Boston, 68 percent were white and 18 percent black.) Seven percent of Republican delegates are Hispanic, compared with 3 percent in 1996 (and 12 percent of Democratic delegates this year).
 Twenty-seven percent of the delegates said they were millionaires, compared with 14 percent of the delegates at the Democratic convention last month in Boston.
 A quarter of them are members of the National Rifle Association, while only 9 percent of the voters are, and 45 percent own a gun, while 30 percent of all voters do.

HEADLINE: Recapturing an Era Paved With Silver

BYLINE: By PAUL LAROCCO

 BODY:
THE rich silver manufacturing history of Meriden and Wallingford exists today only in remnants, in pieces in storage and in the stories from the ever-dwindling number of people who are familiar with its heyday. 
 ''The sign on the highway when you used to drive into Meriden would say, 'Welcome to the Silver City of the World,''' said Bob Johnston, a 77-year-old former sales representative for the International Silver Company, one of the nation's largest silver manufacturers for most of the 20th century. ''Through the years though, by attrition, these places all dried up and really, there's very little in that area these days. It's just a shame.''
 A small group of local historians agree and want to bring silver back to the forefront. Members of the Wallingford Historic Preservation Trust and silver enthusiasts hope to open a new American Silver Museum. They have already put a million dollars into an old home on Main Street in Wallingford. 
 ''I just feel it's so important we maintain that history for our children,'' said Ellen Mandes, a member of the trust's board, and an avid collector who has donated hundreds of silver pieces and Victorian-era furniture for the museum's anticipated opening. ''So many people have no idea about some of the things that were made right here. They've never seen an ice cream fork, or a muddler, or anything like that.''
 A muddler is a small spoon with a pointed end that was used for removing tea leaves, but Ms. Mandes said her active role in the silver museum project is not just to teach children about the obscure cutlery once produced in Central Connecticut. The larger motivation is to ensure that the sterling silverware and serving sets that once made their way to European royalty and other famed figures around the world return to their original home to stay.
 In the late 1980's, Ms. Mandes was part of a group that raised nearly $4 million dollars to erect the Meriden American Silver Museum in the city's downtown. After opening to much hoopla in 1994, the museum lasted barely two years before folding because of a lack of money. A struggle then ensued in which collectors from Providence, R.I., attempted to get the museum's collection moved there for that city's own silver museum efforts. The threat of losing the collection seemed to boost everyone's resolve to find a new home that would last.
 ''When I first moved here,'' Ms. Mandes said, referring to her arrival in Wallingford from Washington D.C. in 1968, ''There was so much silver here. I had collected thousands of pieces already, but I hadn't seen anything like what was in this area. When they talked about taking the collection to Providence, there was no way at all we were going to allow that to happen.''
 In 1999, after several years of searching for a suitable location, the Wallingford Historic Preservation Trust was given a grand, mid-18th-century Italianate home to relocate the museum, which they dubbed the Franklin & Harriet Johnson Mansion after its original owners. There was one problem, however. The house had been given several facelifts since its 1866 construction and had lost essentially all of its Victorian era charm.
 ''Whatever you could do to ruin a house, it was done,'' said John LeTourneau, a trust member who served as director of restoration for the Johnson Mansion. ''But it still had its basic integrity, which was important.''
 Bolstered by a $500,000 grant from the State Department of Economic and Community Development in early 2002, work began in earnest on the home. With several hundred thousand dollars more in donations, the house was restored to its original state, with a period-appropriate staircase, moldings, a new cupola, and the trust's best guess of the home's original color: cream yellow and avocado green. But necessary work to make the home a state-approved museum site handicap accessibility, a state-of-the-art security system and modern electrical wiring drained the trust of its funds to finish the project on the fast-track timeline it had hoped.
 Outside the home recently, a billboard used for an old fund-raiser rested against the wall. It showed a thermometer marked in dollars already raised, reading $800,000. The top of the glass bulb read $1.2 million, however. Inside, century-old, unfinished furniture rested unassembled on the floor. The walls were bare of any decor, and nicked and scuffed floors were coated with sawdust. 
 The home, even with a full, hand-carved 1847 Rogers Brothers tea set on display that morning, looked nowhere near ready for the public's eyes. But the trust president, Jerry Farrell Jr., said that he still hopes to hold some sort of grand opening this fall, although the museum is still well short of the $500,000 it estimates it needs for carpeting, wallpaper and a full exterior landscaping (the grounds are still dirt and gravel) appropriate to the period.
 ''If people came into this fully-built, fully-decorated place, with every piece of silver that ever existed already on display,'' Mr. Farrell said, ''they'd say, 'Why do you need our help?'''
 A fellow trust member, Candice Brashears, said, ''The house here, in the state it's in now, provides people with a good community opportunity to engage in what we're doing.''
 Ms. Mandes, asked later about the trust's financial situation, was more blunt.
 ''We desperately need money,'' she said.
 One thing the trust has going for it, however, is a team of volunteers, led by Mr. Farrell, which has gathered from around the world thousands of pieces of silver produced in Meriden and Wallingford during its glory days. In addition, the group has located invaluable historical perspective from former area silver workers like Mr. Johnston, and the 83-year-old Bill Toth.
 Mr. Toth, who entered the silver industry as an apprentice at R. Wallace & Sons Company in 1939, was one of the last employees of what became Wallace Silversmith Inc. (International Silver merged with Wallace in 1984, and worked as one company in Wallingford until Boston's Syratech Corporation purchased the company and moved operations in the early 1990's.) Even after the firm shut the company down locally, however, Mr. Toth continued working out of Wallingford as an analyst, before retiring a decade ago.
 ''For a while we were all worried where we were going to end up,'' Mr. Toth said. ''Everything was leaving.''
 In his 54 years with Wallace, the Wallingford resident designed flatware patterns that became national bestsellers, watched his plant produce machine gun clips during World War II, and over all, watched his hometown go from the center of the silver world to an industrial ghost town.
 Mr. Johnston recently sent the trust in Wallingford what he calls ''one of the larger silver libraries in the country'' more than 100 volumes of scholarly texts and auction catalogues.
 ''This is a project that excites me,'' said Mr. Johnston, who lives in Baltimore and is preparing for retirement from the private silver appraisal business he runs. ''I was on the board for the one in Meriden that closed, but these days, there are a lot of ways to promote a museum, and I think there will be a market.''

HEADLINE: An Encore Effort to Bring Shakespeare Back to Stratford

BYLINE: By ALISON LEIGH COWAN

DATELINE: STRATFORD, Conn. 

 BODY:
Long before Indian-sponsored casinos became the biggest reason people traveled to Connecticut, the state's biggest draws were Mystic Seaport and Shakespeare, as performed here. 
 From its perch overlooking the Housatonic River and the Long Island Sound, the Shakespeare theater in Stratford offered world-class productions in a grand setting. Schoolchildren came by the busload to see their first serious play and experience small pleasures like a garden featuring 81 plants mentioned in Shakespeare's work. Wealthy patrons came by yacht. Wandering minstrels serenaded the crowd, and picnic blankets were as inescapable as coolers at a tailgate party. 
 But the theater's finances were always shaky, even when Katharine Hepburn and James Earl Jones were filling all 1,500 seats, and the place has been dark for 15 years. 
 The Stratford Festival Theater Inc., has controlled the theater since 1995. It received money from the state, including a $500,000 grant and a $1.5 million loan guarantee, to restore the theater in 1999, but it ran out of money before the work was done. 
 The festival group did make a dent. New carpeting and seats await installation. The stage has been mechanized and the building winterized. A rack of donated clothes stands ready to be turned into costumes. 
 ''That's how close we came,'' said Robert L. Richard, a retired engineer who oversaw repairs and tended the building for Stratford Festival Theater.
 Close enough in another way: the stage was extended, in the shape of a prow, into the audience. That was for a 2000 production of ''The Tempest'' that never opened.
 Now, renovation is at a standstill and theater supporters are pinning their hopes on Connecticut's new governor, M. Jodi Rell, asking her to invoke the state's legal right to seize the property. Many believe that unless the state acts, and then sets aside $5 million to $7 million for restoration, there is little chance of bringing Shakespeare back here any time soon. The town is angling to gain control of the property if the state seizes it, but officials are having trouble persuading the state to give it to the town free of liens and with few restrictions on future use. The executive director of the Stratford Chamber of Commerce, Laura Hoydick, said the town coveted the site because of ''the history and beauty of the theater. 
 ''It's something that is unique to Stratford and still has people remembering it 25 to 30 years later,'' she said. 
 One reason may be that the signs directing drivers to the site of the Stratford Festival Theater are still prominent on nearby highways and roads. But anyone who follows the signs is met by a boarded-up theater on an unkempt 12-acre lot.
 ''It's a shame,'' said Charles Cornelius, a Stratford resident, as he drove through the property recently. His father-in-law was one of the carpenters who built the place.
 Mr. Richard, the engineer who worked on the restoration, put it bluntly: ''We need a deep pocket.''
 The theater was founded in 1955 by Lawrence Langner, a patent lawyer and theatrical producer. In 1931, he founded the Westport Country Playhouse. The Shakespeare Festival Theater opened in Stratford with a performance of ''Julius Caesar,'' joining the ranks of stages in England and Canada where Shakespeare's work is celebrated. 
 Mr. Langner's ambitions were as grand as the space. He modeled the octagonal building after the Globe Theater in England, and arranged for France to donate the teak. And by his death in 1962, the theater was a showcase for talent, new and old.
 Christopher Plummer was Marc Antony in the inaugural production of ''Julius Caesar,'' sharing the stage with Jack Palance as Cassius, Roddy McDowell as Octavius, Raymond Massey as Brutus, and Jerry Stiller as Trinculo. Years later, in 1981, Mr. Plummer packed the house again as Iago in a production of ''Othello'' starring James Earl Jones that moved to Broadway.
 John Houseman was an early artistic director. Bert Lahr was Bottom in ''A Midsummer Night's Dream'' in 1958.
 Katharine Hepburn made herself so at home between performances that she was given a cottage on the grounds. 
 Despite the star-studded productions, the theater nearly always lost money. Joseph Verner Reed, a businessman and diplomat who supported the arts, covered the deficits until his death in 1973. Unable to pay its bills after that, it went dark once, reopened and then filed for bankruptcy protection in 1982.
 To keep the bank from foreclosing in 1983, the state used about $1 million in funds dedicated to the preservation of open space. That turned the theater into a public park under the stewardship of the state's Department of Environmental Protection. Save for a brief last-gasp season in 1989, the theater has been under wraps ever since..
 But there are memories and mementos. Lucille Nichols, historian for the theater, has assembled a multivolume history of the theater from old programs, photographs and other artifacts. State Senator George Gunther of Stratford said state officials told him not to expect much financial support from the state, given his opposition to former Gov. John G. Rowland's dashed efforts to lure the Patriots to Hartford with the promise of a new football stadium.
 But officials with the state's economic development office pointed out the state's contribution in 1999, during the second of Governor Rowland's three terms. 
 ''People might question us putting more money into something that has not been working,'' said Richard J. LoPresti, director of the state's office of business and industry development. ''Thus far, there's been no return on our investment.''

 Lucille Nichols of the Stratford Shakespeare Festival Theater with a photo of Katherine Hepburn performing in ''The Merchant of Venice.'' (Photographs by Douglas Healey for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Off-Court Achievements at the U.S. Open

BYLINE: By Lisa Belkin. 
This column about the intersection of jobs and personal lives appears every other week. E-mail: Belkin@nytimes.com.

 BODY:
DAWN SCHAEFER doesn't care much for tennis. But she will travel to Flushing nearly every morning of the next two weeks to spend up to eight hours a day at the United States Open tennis tournament. 
 If you find your way to the ''food village'' there (to nosh on a $7.50 hamburger, perhaps?) you might see her. She'll be wearing her uniform -- black pants, a navy golf shirt and a black cap that says Restaurant Associates. She got her job through the YAI/National Institute for Disabilities Network (YAI once was an abbreviation for the Young Adult Institute, a forerunner organization), and its subdivision, the Corporate Source, which provides training and work opportunities for adults with learning and developmental impairments. 
 Restaurant Associates, which operates the food village at the Open every year, has been including the disabled among its employees there for the last six years. This time around, it will have 50 of them (last year it was 40) among the 1,400 staff members cooking, serving and cleaning. 
 The benefit to the disabled workers is as concrete as a paycheck and as intangible as self-esteem, says Joel Levy, chief executive of YAI. ''You cannot overestimate the importance of a job to our people.'' Ninety-two percent of Americans with developmental disabilities are unemployed, he says. In New York State, 84 percent of people with disabilities in a vocational program like the one he runs have no jobs.
 The disabled workers at the Open, he says, know they are lucky. ''Most people make money and they don't enjoy what they do. Our employees enjoy the fact that they have a job.'' 
 The companies hiring the disabled also benefit. The simplest entry-level jobs -- cleaning tables or refilling ketchup dispensers -- are tough to keep filled, Dr. Levy says. ''But this population does very well at repetitive tasks. They don't get bored.'' 
 That is particularly important at this year's Open. 
 ''With the Republican National Convention coming at the same time as the Open, we had to scramble to cobble together a work force,'' says Michael Sullivan, vice president for operations for the sports and entertainment division of Restaurant Associates. (By the by, Mr. Sullivan defends the price of his $7.50 hamburgers, describing them as ''Certified Angus Beef, grilled to order.'') The jobs the YAI workers fill pay $8 an hour or less, and ''if I had to go out on the street and fill that job classification, I would not get nearly the same kind of focused work force,'' Mr. Sullivan says. 
 To qualify for these jobs, YAI employees attend training programs throughout the year. ''They teach us that you shouldn't come to work late, you shouldn't steal on the job,'' Ms. Schaefer said. Of course, she knew this before she took her class, she said. Things she didn't know included ''how to clock in and out.'' 
 Ms. Schaefer was one of a small group of YAI-trained workers I spoke to as they prepared to start work tomorrow. All but Ms. Schaefer have worked at the Open in other years. They do not mind the simplicity of their jobs, they told me. They are relieved that the jobs are ''easy'' and say they find the work ''fun.'' When they wear uniforms, customers think they have the answers, a feeling they enjoy. ''They ask me, 'Where is the ketchup? Where is the mustard?''' said Byron Freeman, 20. ''And I tell them.'' 
 Unlike Ms. Schaefer, Mr. Freeman is a tennis fan. ''I know the Williams sisters and Agassi and Andy Roddick,'' he said, noting that his favorites are not likely to come to the food village. They aren't the only ones who will be dining elsewhere. ''We don't eat the same food as the customers,'' Mr. Freeman said. Is the employee food better or worse than the Certified Angus burgers? Mr. Freeman giggled, but did not answer. 
 The employees all say that while they are grateful for this job, it is not their dream. ''If I had a choice, I would work in a hospital, helping people,'' said Peter Figueroa, 40. 
 Mr. Freeman would be a cashier if he could, he said, because ''I like to work with money.'' Boyd Cephus, 37, would ''work with old folks -- you know, older than me.'' Ms. Schaefer would be a registered nurse. She has taken a class to be a certified home health aide but she has not been able to find a job. In fact, punching the time clock at the Open will lead to her very first paycheck.
 All these workers know exactly what they will do with the money they make. Mr. Figueroa and Mr. Cephus will put theirs in the bank. Mr. Freeman's mother will take him to cash his check. ''Then I get to buy whatever I want,'' he said. 
 And Ms. Schaefer? She blushed when she answered. She is going to be married July 4, at City Hall. She is saving to buy a beautiful white dress.

HEADLINE: The Price Is Still Right

BYLINE: By MERRI ROSENBERG. 
wested@nytimes.com

DATELINE: VALHALLA 

 BODY:
RAISE the cost of anything by 20 percent, and one would expect some gripes from customers.
 But when the county's Board of Legislators recently approved a 20.6 percent tuition increase at Westchester Community College here, bringing the annual tuition to $2,950 from $2,450, there was scarcely a peep.
 ''Surprisingly enough, we haven't heard anything from anybody,'' said Tere Wisell, director of admissions at Westchester Community College. ''We thought there might be some impact, but it's still such an affordable opportunity.'' 
 Perhaps it helped that students had known about the increase since late June, when the college's trustees voted to raise tuition, and the $500 annual additional payment had already been factored into tuition bills for the term that starts Sept. 7.
 ''We already went through the process, when the board of trustees voted in the tuition hike months ago,'' said Patrick Hennessey, director of college relations for the college. ''Students have been paying for fall registration as of last month.''
 One change during the past several years, Ms. Wisell suggested, has been a slight shift in the composition of the student body. What had begun nearly six decades ago as a higher education institution primarily for nontraditional students, like older students, single parents and workers who needed to gain additional skills, has increasingly become the college of choice for high school graduates.
 ''In recent years, the perception of W.C.C. has shifted somewhat,'' said Ms. Wisell. ''It's the college to which most Westchester high school graduates go.''
 For all that the county would seem to be the domain of those who choose big-name colleges and universities, the reality is that there is a large universe of students for whom cost and value come into play. High school graduates from affluent communities like Scarsdale, Chappaqua and Bronxville high schools are more than offset by graduates from Yonkers, New Rochelle, Mount Vernon and White Plains. And economics clearly plays a significant role even for many students who might be able to afford other schools.
 Hyun Tai Lew, for example, a second-year liberal arts and social sciences major from Scarsdale, said, ''W.C.C. is one of the cheapest rates for me.'' He plans to transfer to Wesleyan or New York University after he completes his associate's degree at the college. Many students who start at W.C.C. complete their education at Ivy League universities like Columbia, Cornell and Yale, or selective campuses like Stanford, as well as the state's four-year colleges.
 As Joseph Hankin, the president of the college, said: ''Fifty-four percent of all college students now start at community colleges. It's becoming more of a trend nationally. In the past, when we raised tuition by $100, we lost 1 percent of the enrollment. When tuition was raised about three years ago, there was no impact. It's still the best bargain around.''
 The college has about 12,000 students enrolled in for-credit programs, with an equivalent number who take non-credit continuing education courses. Its annual budget of $97.2 million is provided by the state, the county and student tuition payments; for the 2004-5 academic year, the county's share of the college's budget is $19.1 million, reflecting a subsidy of about 5.2 percent. The State Legislature has approved a per student subsidy of $50 at the state's community colleges. If that measure is not vetoed by the governor, the college would receive an additional $440,000 for its budget. Some staff positions remain unfilled and will depend on state financing and enrollment.
 The college also has increased its allocation of scholarships during the past few years, said Shirley Contino-Phillips, vice president of external affairs and director of the WCC Foundation. ''We give out about $750,000 a year, with the average scholarship about $1,500,'' she said. About 500 students receive scholarships annually. 
 ''I'm on financial aid, so am covered,'' said Nichole Justice-Hylton, a second-year student in the restaurant management program. Ms. Justice-Hylton, who is a student representative to the board of trustees, is a New Rochelle mother of two, attends school full time and works as a caterer. ''Students were in line with the increase, because we knew it wasn't done haphazardly or taken very lightly.'' Still, she said, ''We would rather that the county and state would pull their weight.''

HEADLINE: Shop, Walk, Work And Demonstrate

BYLINE: By LISA KELLER. 
Lisa Keller, an associate professor of history and director of the journalism program at Purchase College of the State University of New York, is writing a book about public order in 19th-century New York and London.

DATELINE: PURCHASE 

 BODY:
IN 1790, while New York City was still the capital of the United States, the first Congress passed the Bill of Rights, affirming our basic liberties as a free people: ''Congress shall make no lawabridging the freedom of speech, or of the press; or the right of the people peaceably to assemble.''
 The concept is simple, but the practice is complicated. In fact, there hasn't been an unlimited right to assemble in New York City for 130 years. 
 The current controversy about where protestors can demonstrate during the Republican National Convention, which kicks off tomorrow, is just the latest manifestation of a conflict that has been dividing the city for more than a century. In most big cities, it is difficult to find places to parade or to protest without interfering with the rights and needs of other residents. In New York City, where traffic, crowds and commerce are particularly intense, it is a near miracle that any kind of large public assembly is ever held. We demand to have our say anywhere and any time on any subject (particularly when we don't like something), but we also want to be able to shop, walk, work, travel and eat without disruption. 
 After the Civil War, New York became one of the first cities in the nation to require permits for individuals and groups seeking to use streets and parks for assembly or marches. Bitter lessons had been learned from the 1863 Draft Riots, which left more than 100 people dead and turned the city into chaos, and the 1871 Irish Orange Riots, which left more than 40 dead. The city decided it would allow public assembly only in controlled circumstances.
 The sources for that control lay not in the courts but with the city's municipal authorities -- mainly municipal agencies, politicians, police and influential citizens' groups -- representing a broad class, ethnic and religious spectrum. Their agenda was to keep order in the city, allowing it to function without economic disruption and preventing violence and loss of life.
 The timing of the decision to require permits in the early 1870's was no accident. Socialists, trade unionists and unemployed workers sought to redress economic and political grievances on city streets, and looked across the ocean to the model set by Londoners, who had seized Trafalgar Square and Hyde Park as the seats of vox populi. 
 The New York solution was a compromise reached after trial and error. It allowed some parks to be used, but changed that decision after excessive wear and tear. After the 1874 ''Blood or Bread'' melee in Tompkins Square Park, the city closed down the square as a site of protest for many decades. 
 However, in 1896, when a neighborhood association complained about the noise and disruption from Italian street parades, the police turned a deaf ear -- the parade organizers had legal permits. City authorities concluded that if they could control circumstances by setting clear conditions for meeting times and places through the permit, then disturbances to public peace, injury to individuals and interruptions to traffic and commerce could be avoided, while still allowing free assembly. Each permit request was individually determined, but no place and no time frame was ever absolute or guaranteed for public assembly. 
 The public during this period in the late 1800's had little legal recourse if denied a permit except to take to the streets and face arrest. 
 The turbulent 1920's and 1930's saw a proliferation of demonstrations and marches and much more debate over public assembly. In 1941, Robert Moses, who was parks commissioner at the time, banned a protest in Union Square Park, which prompted the New York Civil Liberties Committee to charge that he was changing city policy about demonstrations, misinterpreting the law and abridging the right of free speech. Citing a Supreme Court decision, Mr. Moses responded that the right to use a park was not absolute, especially if there was a likelihood of riot or fatalities. It was an old policy, he reminded the committee, and the city had usually managed to accommodate requests for public meetings.
 In 2004, a persistent fear of terrorism has added a new wrinkle to the long conflict between freedom and order in great cities. But we must remember that democracy is messy and that free speech is meaningless if we can only shout at 500 empty acres. We must accept the likelihood that some people will be late for work, some people will miss dinner, some grass will be trampled and some people will sit in snarled traffic. The founders of our republic knew that freedom and convenience did not go hand in hand, and they knew the consequences of limiting the right of assembly. 
 If we lose the right to speak our minds in Manhattan this week, then the city won't be worth the $24 that the early Dutch settlers allegedly paid the Indians for it in 1626.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

DOCUMENT-TYPE: Op-Ed

PUBLICATION-TYPE: Newspaper

SUBJECT: PROTESTS & DEMONSTRATIONS (90%); CONFERENCES & CONVENTIONS (90%); RIOTS (89%); FREEDOM OF ASSEMBLY & ASSOCIATION (78%); CONSTITUTIONAL LAW (78%); CITY LIFE (78%); LEGISLATION (78%); US REPUBLICAN PARTY (78%); CITY GOVERNMENT (78%); UNEMPLOYED PERSONS (77%); POLITICS (75%); PARADES & MARCHES (73%); HOMEOWNERS ASSOCIATIONS (73%); FREEDOM OF SPEECH (73%); CAMPAIGNS & ELECTIONS (73%); ELECTIONS (73%); POLITICAL PARTY CONVENTIONS (70%); US REPUBLICAN NATIONAL CONVENTIONS (70%); NOISE POLLUTION (65%); LABOR UNIONS (51%) Presidential Election of 2004; Demonstrations and Riots; Freedom of Assembly; Conventions, National (US)

HEADLINE: Staten Islanders' Tough Commutes

 BODY:

To the Editor:
 In ''Help for the M.T.A.'' (editorial, Aug. 15), you rightly state that the Metropolitan Transportation Authority should focus on basic needs first. Under the advance plans proposed by the authority, the plight of Staten Islanders in particular will become unbearable.
 Staten Islanders face one of the longest commutes of any community in America, in many cases up to two hours each way by express bus. For many Staten Islanders the express buses to Manhattan are the only way to get to work, and raising these fares by 50 percent will surely cripple the economy on the island.
 In my own case, the combination of extremely long commuting hours and the very high commuting costs will tip the scales in favor of moving out of the island to one of the other boroughs. I am sure that my situation is not unique.

Ramnath Mahadevan
Manor Heights, Staten Island

HEADLINE: The Weak Dollar And the Price of Oil

 BODY:

To the Editor:
 Re ''The Peril That Trails an Oil Shock'' (Gretchen Morgenson, Aug. 22), which looked at the potential economic damage from high oil prices:
 When it comes to the rising price of oil, the elephant in the room is the ever-weakening United States dollar. The persistent deficits (trade, current account, budget) are eating away at the strength of our currency. 
 Certainly, increased global demand for oil, threats of disruption to its supply and speculative buying are relevant causes for the climb in oil, but until this country begins to address the pervasiveness of debt in our economy stemming from the deficits and the easy-money policies of the Federal Reserve, then the dollar will continue to weaken and we will be forced to grapple with issues like the rising cost of commodities.

Jeffrey B. Diamond
Rye Brook, N.Y., Aug. 22

HEADLINE: SOME SUFFER MORE

 BODY:
The ebb tide of the 2001 recession and its aftermath didn't strand all boats equally, the government reported Thursday. Figures for 2003 released by the United States Census Bureau indicated that income inequality has grown since 2002. 
 In 2003, the bureau reported, the number of people considered poor and those without health insurance grew for the third straight year, and families near the bottom and middle were suffering the most.
 Some other inequities: In the South, the nation's poorest region, as well as in cities and among immigrants, pay didn't keep pace with inflation; single-parent families were the ones in which poverty rose most sharply; the gap in wages between men and women grew, and health-insurance coverage fell only for families earning less than $75,000 a year.

HEADLINE: A Declining Budget For Westchester Magnet

BYLINE: By MERRI ROSENBERG

 BODY:
The loss of a $ 650,000 grant from the United States Department of Education means that the Westchester Magnet Academy, an alternative-type high school that serves 175 students from the Greenburgh Central 7, Harrison and Irvington school districts, will start the school year with a 25 percent decline in its operating budget, leading to cost-cutting measures in programming and staffing.
 The school, which opened in 2001 under the terms of a three-year, $2 million federal grant to bring together students from different ethnic and economic backgrounds, offers a Regents-based academic curriculum combined with hands-on activities and projects. 
 Without the grant, Westchester Magnet will be financed by the participating districts. The school also receives $300,000 from a state grant.
 There are other uncertainties as well. Although the program is currently situated on the Manhattanville College campus in Purchase, the college's trustees voted in June to end its relationship with the Westchester Magnet Academy after the current academic year.
 The school is working with Congresswoman Nita M. Lowey's office in an effort to restore some of the federal money, as well as seeking private donations and other grants, said Peter Lisi, assistant superintendent of the Greenburgh Central 7 school district.
 ''It's a very successful program,'' Mr. Lisi said. ''It offers an experiential approach to learning and the results have been very positive.'' MERRI ROSENBERG

HEADLINE: Paid Notice: Deaths
BERKOWITZ, WILLIAM

 BODY:
 BERKOWITZ--William. Age 62, died peacefully in his sleep at his home in Sarasota, FL, on Sunday, August 22, 2004, after a long battle with cancer. Mr. Berkowitz, as President of one of the nation's first and largest retail Money Market Mutual Funds, Dreyfus Liquid Assets, helped to transform the way Americans save. As he told ''Forbes'' in 1980, ''...the savings public is becoming more sophisticated. It will not stand for this 5% rate on passbooks (savings accounts).'' He fought against the regulation of the money market industry and welcomed competition from banks by testifying in favor of lifting Regulation Q--which prevented banks from paying prevailing high interest rates on savings accounts. In 1964 a twenty-three year old William ''Bill'' Berkowitz came to work at The Dreyfus Corporation, the New York based investment management firm. By 1974 Bill Berkowitz had become an officer of The Dreyfus Corporation, and soon thereafter he created and became President of Dreyfus Liquid Assets, one of the first retail oriented Money Market Mutual Funds. Dreyfus' money market services and assets grew to a top industry position under his leadership. By 1981 most of Dreyfus' $12.4 billion of assets under management was in short-term money in over 300,000 accounts. Bill became President of the Reich & Tang, L.P. Mutual Funds Group and Executive Vice President and Director of Reich & Tang L.P. in 1982. The Mutual Funds Group grew rapidly under his leadership until 1994 when Reich & Tang, L.P. merged with the asset management division of the New England Companies, creating one of the largest investment management companies in the world. 
He was also a VicePresident and Board member of various third party mutual funds like Gabelli Asset Growth, Equitable Life's money market mutual funds and Speers, Benzak, Solomon and Farrell Funds. He was born September 20, 1941, in Bronx, NY. Before receiving his Bachelor of Science in Finance from New York University in 1963, he attended Syracuse University and graduated from Stuyvesant High School in Manhattan in 1959. He lived in Middletown, NJ, and commuted to New York City for 16 years before moving to Sarasota, FL, in 1982. He retired from Reich and Tang, L.P. in 1994. Through the William and Dale S. Berkowitz Family Foundation and through invididual efforts, Bill supported the Flanzer Jewish Community Center of Sarasota, FL, and its Maccabi Games. He also supported the Florida Studio Theatre and the Sarasota YMCA Foundation. Bill was an avid art collector and a patron of a number of local Sarasota artists. Survivors include his beloved wife of 41 years, Dale S.; a daughter and son in law, Dana B. and John Snyder of Columbia, MD; sons and daughters in law David L. and Patti C. of Middletown, NJ, Barry J. and Chrissy of Potomac, MD, and Jeffrey A. and Susan of Sarasota; a sister Harriet Fortgang of Atlanta; and six grandchildren, Matthew, Dylan, Daniel, Bryce, Anna and Alexandra. Services and entombment were held on August 25, 2004, at the Palms Funeral Home and Memorial Park, 170 Honore Ave, Sarasota, FL 34232. Memorial donations may be made to Hospice of Southwest Florida, 5955 Rand Blvd, Sarasota, FL 34228.

HEADLINE: A Slowdown And Why It Matters

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

 BODY:
ECONOMISTS expect new data for August to show a slight slowdown in the manufacturing sector, which has been growing rapidly for nine months.
 The Institute for Supply Management's manufacturing index, due on Wednesday, is expected to slip to 60 from 62 in July, according to the consensus forecast from Bloomberg News. Because a reading of more than 50 generally means that the manufacturing sector is growing, a slip to 60 may not seem worrisome. 
 But while manufacturing accounts for only a small portion of overall economic growth, it plays an important role in some toss-up states in the presidential election campaign, which goes into full swing after the Republican National Convention this week.
 In addition, there are some signs, according to economists at Goldman Sachs, that manufacturing growth may slow further.
 Jan Hatzius, a senior economist at Goldman Sachs, said in a report to clients that Goldman expected the manufacturing index to fall to the mid-50's by the end of this year -- and maybe even lower next year.
 He bases that forecast on the performance of the inventory-to-sales ratio for durable goods. If this ratio is falling, inventories are declining relative to sales, meaning that production is too slow for current consumer demand and should increase, he said.
 When this ratio is rising, inventories are increasing relative to sales, meaning production is too high and consumer demand should slow.
 The July durable-goods data, released last week, show that the inventory-to-sales ratio is still declining, compared with its level in July a year ago.
 But the rate of the year-over-year declines has shrunk significantly, leading Mr. Hatzius to conclude that the pace of manufacturing growth will be slowing.
 The 4.7 percent decline in the ratio for July, compared with July 2003, is smaller than the declines of 8.2 percent for June and 10.3 percent for May. The July reading, he said, is consistent with a manufacturing-index level of about 54.

EMPLOYMENT WATCH -- Job growth in August, which will be reported on Friday, is expected to be way up from July, when only 32,000 jobs were created. But the Bloomberg forecast of 150,000 new payroll jobs is still below the monthly average of 177,000 this year.
 The predictions for August range from a high of 250,000 to a low of 50,000. For July, when the actual number was 32,000, the lowest forecast was 140,000. It is not clear what kind of impact there will be from Hurricane Charley, which ripped through parts of Florida at the end of the survey week for the August unemployment report.
 The jobs data comes out the morning after President Bush is scheduled to accept his party's nomination at the convention. Even with 250,000 new jobs in August, Mr. Bush would still need 919,000 more jobs by year-end to avoid a decline in employment during his current term.
